<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Hepatitis</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><table class="infobox" style="width:22em">

<th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold;background-color: lightgrey">Hepatitis</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alcoholic_hepatitis.jpg"><img alt="Alcoholic hepatitis.jpg" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="768" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Alcoholic_hepatitis.jpg/280px-Alcoholic_hepatitis.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Alcoholic_hepatitis.jpg/420px-Alcoholic_hepatitis.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Alcoholic_hepatitis.jpg/560px-Alcoholic_hepatitis.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a>
<div><a href="/wiki/Alcoholic_liver_disease" title="Alcoholic liver disease">Alcoholic hepatitis</a> evident by fatty changes, <a href="/wiki/Necrosis" title="Necrosis">cell death</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mallory_bodies" title="Mallory bodies">Mallory bodies</a></div>
</td>
</tr>

</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Symptoms</th>
<td><a href="/wiki/Jaundice" title="Jaundice">yellowish skin</a>, poor appetite, abdominal pain<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MedLine2016_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-MedLine2016-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2016QA_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2016QA-2">[2]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Duration</th>
<td>short term or long term<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MedLine2016_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-MedLine2016-1">[1]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Causes</th>
<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viruses" title="Viruses">viruses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">alcohol</a>, toxins, autoimmune<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2016QA_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2016QA-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2016_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2016-3">[3]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Prevention</th>
<td>vaccination<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2016QA_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2016QA-2">[2]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Treatment</th>
<td>medication, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liver_transplant" title="Liver transplant">liver transplant</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MedLine2016_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-MedLine2016-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trans2012_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Trans2012-4">[4]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Frequency</th>
<td>&gt; 500 million cases<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2016_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2016-3">[3]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Deaths</th>
<td>&gt; one million a year<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2016_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2016-3">[3]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
<tr class="noprint">
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
<div style="text-align: right;">[<a class="extiw" href="https://www.wikidata.org/wiki/Q131742" title="d:Q131742">edit on Wikidata</a>]</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>Hepatitis</b> is <a href="/wiki/Inflammation" title="Inflammation">inflammation</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">liver</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2016_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2016-3">[3]</a></sup> Some people have no symptoms whereas others develop <a href="/wiki/Jaundice" title="Jaundice">yellow discoloration of the skin and whites of the eyes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anorexia_(symptom)" title="Anorexia (symptom)">poor appetite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vomiting" title="Vomiting">vomiting</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatigue_(medicine)" title="Fatigue (medicine)">tiredness</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abdominal_pain" title="Abdominal pain">abdominal pain</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Diarrhea" title="Diarrhea">diarrhea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MedLine2016_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-MedLine2016-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2016QA_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2016QA-2">[2]</a></sup> Hepatitis may be temporary (acute) or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chronic_disease" title="Chronic disease">long term</a> (chronic) depending on if it lasts for less than or more than six months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MedLine2016_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-MedLine2016-1">[1]</a></sup> Acute hepatitis can sometimes <a href="/wiki/Self-limiting_(biology)" title="Self-limiting (biology)">resolve on its own</a>, progress to chronic hepatitis, or rarely result in <a href="/wiki/Acute_liver_failure" title="Acute liver failure">acute liver failure</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Over time the chronic form may progress to <a href="/wiki/Cirrhosis" title="Cirrhosis">scarring of the liver</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liver_failure" title="Liver failure">liver failure</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Liver_cancer" title="Liver cancer">liver cancer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2016_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2016-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>The most common cause worldwide is <a href="/wiki/Viral_hepatitis" title="Viral hepatitis">viruses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2016QA_2-4"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2016QA-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2016_3-5"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2016-3">[3]</a></sup> Other causes include <a href="/wiki/Alcoholism" title="Alcoholism">heavy alcohol use</a>, certain medications, toxins, other infections, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autoimmune_diseases" title="Autoimmune diseases">autoimmune diseases</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2016QA_2-5"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2016QA-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2016_3-6"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2016-3">[3]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-alcoholic_steatohepatitis" title="Non-alcoholic steatohepatitis">non-alcoholic steatohepatitis</a> (NASH).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASH2014_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASH2014-7">[7]</a></sup> There are five main types of viral hepatitis: <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_A" title="Hepatitis A">type A</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_B" title="Hepatitis B">B</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_C" title="Hepatitis C">C</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_D" title="Hepatitis D">D</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_E" title="Hepatitis E">E</a>. Hepatitis A and E are mainly spread by contaminated food and water. Hepatitis B is mainly <a href="/wiki/Sexually_transmitted_infection" title="Sexually transmitted infection">sexually transmitted</a>, but may also be <a href="/wiki/Vertically_transmitted_infection" title="Vertically transmitted infection">passed from mother to baby</a> during <a href="/wiki/Pregnancy" title="Pregnancy">pregnancy</a> or <a href="/wiki/Childbirth" title="Childbirth">childbirth</a>. Both hepatitis B and hepatitis C are commonly spread through infected <a href="/wiki/Blood" title="Blood">blood</a> such as may occur during <a href="/wiki/Needle_sharing" title="Needle sharing">needle sharing</a> by <a href="/wiki/Drug_injection" title="Drug injection">intravenous drug users</a>. Hepatitis D can only infect people already infected with hepatitis B.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2016_3-7"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2016-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hepatitis A, B, and D are <a href="/wiki/Vaccine-preventable_diseases" title="Vaccine-preventable diseases">preventable</a> with <a href="/wiki/Immunization" title="Immunization">immunization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2016QA_2-6"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2016QA-2">[2]</a></sup> Medications may be used to treat chronic cases of viral hepatitis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MedLine2016_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-MedLine2016-1">[1]</a></sup> There is no specific treatment for NASH; however, a healthy lifestyle, including physical activity, a <a href="/wiki/Healthy_diet" title="Healthy diet">healthy diet</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Weight_loss" title="Weight loss">weight loss</a>, is important.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASH2014_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASH2014-7">[7]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Autoimmune_hepatitis" title="Autoimmune hepatitis">Autoimmune hepatitis</a> may be treated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immunosuppressants" title="Immunosuppressants">medications to suppress the immune system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liver_transplant" title="Liver transplant">liver transplant</a> may also be an option in certain cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trans2012_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Trans2012-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>Worldwide in 2015, hepatitis A occurred in about 114 million people, chronic hepatitis B affected about 343 million people and chronic hepatitis C about 142 million people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> In the United States, NASH affects about 11 million people and <a href="/wiki/Alcoholic_hepatitis" title="Alcoholic hepatitis">alcoholic hepatitis</a> affects about 5 million people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASH2014_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-NASH2014-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Hepatitis results in more than a million deaths a year, most of which occur indirectly from liver scarring or liver cancer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2016_3-8"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2016-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> In the United States, hepatitis A is estimated to occur in about 2,500 people a year and results in about 75 deaths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> The word is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a> <i>hêpar</i> (<span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">ἧπαρ</span>), meaning "liver", and <i>-itis</i> (<span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">-ῖτις</span>), meaning "inflammation".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Signs_and_symptoms">Signs and symptoms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Signs and symptoms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jaundice_eye.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="501" data-file-width="700" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Jaundice_eye.jpg/290px-Jaundice_eye.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Jaundice_eye.jpg/435px-Jaundice_eye.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Jaundice_eye.jpg/580px-Jaundice_eye.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jaundice_eye.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Jaundiced eyes</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hepatitis has a broad spectrum of presentations that range from a complete lack of symptoms to severe <a href="/wiki/Liver_failure" title="Liver failure">liver failure</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-16">[16]</a></sup> The acute form of hepatitis, generally caused by viral infection, is characterized by <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_symptoms" title="Constitutional symptoms">constitutional symptoms</a> that are typically self-limiting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-15">[15]</a></sup> Chronic hepatitis presents similarly, but can manifest <a href="/wiki/Medical_sign" title="Medical sign">signs</a> and symptoms specific to liver dysfunction with long-standing inflammation and damage to the organ.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Acute_hepatitis">Acute hepatitis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Acute hepatitis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Acute viral hepatitis follows a pattern of infection that involves three distinct phases:</p>
<ol>
<li>The initial <a href="/wiki/Prodrome" title="Prodrome">prodromal phase</a> (preceding symptoms) involves non-specific and flu-like symptoms common to many acute viral infections. These include <a href="/wiki/Fatigue_(medical)" title="Fatigue (medical)">fatigue</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nausea" title="Nausea">nausea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vomiting" title="Vomiting">vomiting</a>, poor appetite, joint pain, and headaches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-15">[15]</a></sup> Fever, when present, is most common in cases of hepatitis A and E.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup> Late in this phase, people can experience liver-specific symptoms, including <a href="/wiki/Choluria" title="Choluria">choluria</a> (dark urine) and clay-colored stools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-4"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-:0-15">[15]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jaundice" title="Jaundice">Yellowing of the skin and whites of the eyes</a> follow the prodrome after about 1–2 weeks and can last for up to 4 weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-5"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_15-4"><a href="#cite_note-:0-15">[15]</a></sup> The non-specific symptoms seen in the prodromal typically resolve by this time, but people will develop an <a href="/wiki/Hepatomegaly" title="Hepatomegaly">enlarged liver</a> and right upper abdominal pain or discomfort.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-6"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup> 10–20% of people will also experience an <a href="/wiki/Splenomegaly" title="Splenomegaly">enlarged spleen</a>, while some people will also experience a mild unintentional weight loss.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-7"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-:2-16">[16]</a></sup></li>
<li>The recovery phase is characterized by resolution of the clinical symptoms of hepatitis with persistent elevations in <a href="/wiki/Liver_function_tests" title="Liver function tests">liver lab values</a> and potentially a persistently enlarged liver.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-8"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup> All cases of hepatitis A and E are expected to fully resolve after 1–2 months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-9"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup> Most hepatitis B cases are also self-limiting and will resolve in 3–4 months. Few cases of hepatitis C will resolve completely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-10"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup></li>
</ol>
<p>Both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drug-induced_hepatitis" title="Drug-induced hepatitis">drug-induced hepatitis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Autoimmune_hepatitis" title="Autoimmune hepatitis">autoimmune hepatitis</a> can present very similarly to acute viral hepatitis, with slight variations in symptoms depending on the cause.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-19">[19]</a></sup> Cases of drug-induced hepatitis can manifest with systemic signs of an allergic reaction including rash, fever, <a href="/wiki/Serositis" title="Serositis">serositis</a> (inflammation of membranes lining certain organs), elevated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eosinophil_granulocyte" title="Eosinophil granulocyte">eosinophils</a> (a type of white blood cell), and <a href="/wiki/Bone_marrow_suppression" title="Bone marrow suppression">suppression of bone marrow activity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fulminant_hepatitis">Fulminant hepatitis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Fulminant hepatitis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Fulminant hepatitis, or massive hepatic <a href="/wiki/Necrosis" title="Necrosis">cell death</a>, is a rare and life-threatening complication of acute hepatitis that can occur in cases of hepatitis B, D, and E, in addition to drug-induced and autoimmune hepatitis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-11"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-:3-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-19">[19]</a></sup> The complication more frequently occurs in instances of hepatitis B and D co-infection at a rate of 2–20% and in pregnant women with hepatitis E at rate of 15–20% of cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-12"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_15-5"><a href="#cite_note-:0-15">[15]</a></sup> In addition to the signs of acute hepatitis, people can also demonstrate signs of <a href="/wiki/Coagulopathy" title="Coagulopathy">coagulopathy</a> (abnormal coagulation studies with easy bruising and bleeding) and <a href="/wiki/Encephalopathy" title="Encephalopathy">encephalopathy</a> (confusion, disorientation, and <a href="/wiki/Somnolence" title="Somnolence">sleepiness</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-13"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_15-6"><a href="#cite_note-:0-15">[15]</a></sup> Mortality due to fulminant hepatitis is typically the result of various complications including <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_edema" title="Cerebral edema">cerebral edema</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gastrointestinal_bleeding" title="Gastrointestinal bleeding">gastrointestinal bleeding</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sepsis" title="Sepsis">sepsis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_failure" title="Respiratory failure">respiratory failure</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Kidney_failure" title="Kidney failure">kidney failure</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-14"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chronic_hepatitis">Chronic hepatitis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Chronic hepatitis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Acute cases of hepatitis are seen to be resolved well within a six-month period.When hepatitis is continued for more than six months it is termed chronic hepatitis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Chronic hepatitis is often asymptomatic early in its course and is detected only by liver laboratory studies for <a href="/wiki/Screening_(medicine)" title="Screening (medicine)">screening</a> purposes or to evaluate non-specific symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_16-3"><a href="#cite_note-:2-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup> As the inflammation progresses, patients can develop constitutional symptoms similar to acute hepatitis, including fatigue, nausea, vomiting, poor appetite, and joint pain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup> Jaundice can occur as well, but much later in the disease process and is typically a sign of advanced disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-3"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup> Chronic hepatitis interferes with hormonal functions of the liver which can result in acne, <a href="/wiki/Hirsutism" title="Hirsutism">hirsutism</a> (abnormal hair growth), and <a href="/wiki/Amenorrhoea" title="Amenorrhoea">amenorrhea</a> (lack of menstrual period) in women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-4"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup> Extensive damage and scarring of the liver over time defines <a href="/wiki/Cirrhosis" title="Cirrhosis">cirrhosis</a>, a condition in which the liver's ability to function is permanently impeded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_16-4"><a href="#cite_note-:2-16">[16]</a></sup> This results in jaundice, weight loss, coagulopathy, <a href="/wiki/Ascites" title="Ascites">ascites</a> (abdominal fluid collection), and <a href="/wiki/Peripheral_edema" title="Peripheral edema">peripheral edema</a> (leg swelling).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-5"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup> Cirrhosis can lead to other life-threatening complications such as <a href="/wiki/Hepatic_encephalopathy" title="Hepatic encephalopathy">hepatic encephalopathy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Esophageal_varices" title="Esophageal varices">esophageal varices</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hepatorenal_syndrome" title="Hepatorenal syndrome">hepatorenal syndrome</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hepatocellular_carcinoma" title="Hepatocellular carcinoma">liver cancer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_16-5"><a href="#cite_note-:2-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes">Causes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Causes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Causes of hepatitis can be divided into the following major categories: infectious, metabolic, ischemic, autoimmune, genetic, and other. Infectious agents include viruses, bacteria, and parasites. <a href="/wiki/Hepatotoxicity" title="Hepatotoxicity">Toxins, drugs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alcoholic_liver_disease" title="Alcoholic liver disease">alcohol</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Non-alcoholic_fatty_liver_disease" title="Non-alcoholic fatty liver disease">non-alcoholic fatty liver disease</a> are metabolic causes of liver injury and inflammation. <a href="/wiki/Autoimmune_hepatitis" title="Autoimmune hepatitis">Autoimmune</a> and genetic causes of hepatitis involve genetic predispositions and tend to affect characteristic populations.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Infectious">Infectious</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Infectious">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Viral_hepatitis">Viral hepatitis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Viral hepatitis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Viral_hepatitis" title="Viral hepatitis">Viral hepatitis</a></div>

<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Viral_hepatitis.webm" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Explanation of viral hepatitis</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Viral_hepatitis" title="Viral hepatitis">Viral hepatitis</a> is the most common type of hepatitis worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Viral hepatitis is caused by five different viruses (hepatitis A, B, C, D, and E).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-15"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_A" title="Hepatitis A">Hepatitis A</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_E" title="Hepatitis E">hepatitis E</a> behave similarly: they are both transmitted by the <a href="/wiki/Fecal%E2%80%93oral_route" title="Fecal–oral route">fecal–oral route</a>, are more common in developing countries, and are self-limiting illnesses that do not lead to chronic hepatitis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-16"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_B" title="Hepatitis B">Hepatitis B</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_C" title="Hepatitis C">hepatitis C</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_D" title="Hepatitis D">hepatitis D</a> are transmitted when blood or <a href="/wiki/Mucous_membrane" title="Mucous membrane">mucous membranes</a> are exposed to infected blood and body fluids, such as semen and vaginal secretions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-17"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup> Viral particles have also been found in saliva and breastmilk. However, kissing, sharing utensils, and breastfeeding do not lead to transmission unless these fluids are introduced into open sores or cuts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hepatitis B and C can present either acutely or chronically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-18"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup> Hepatitis D is a defective virus that requires hepatitis B to replicate and is only found with hepatitis B co-infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-19"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup> In adults, hepatitis B infection is most commonly self-limiting, with less than 5% progressing to chronic state, and 20 to 30% of those chronically infected developing cirrhosis and/or liver cancer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-:8-25">[25]</a></sup> However, infection in infants and children frequently leads to chronic infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-:8-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>Unlike hepatitis B, most cases of hepatitis C lead to chronic infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:30_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-:30-26">[26]</a></sup> Hepatitis C is the second most common cause of cirrhosis in the US (second to alcoholic hepatitis).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:33_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-:33-27">[27]</a></sup> In the 1970s and 1980s, blood transfusions were a major factor in spreading hepatitis C virus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:30_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-:30-26">[26]</a></sup> Since widespread screening of blood products for hepatitis C began in 1992, the risk of acquiring hepatitis C from a blood transfusion has decreased from approximately 10% in the 1970s to 1 in 2 million currently.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-20"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Parasitic_hepatitis">Parasitic hepatitis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Parasitic hepatitis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Echinococcus_granulosus.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Echinococcus_granulosus.JPG/220px-Echinococcus_granulosus.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Echinococcus_granulosus.JPG/330px-Echinococcus_granulosus.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Echinococcus_granulosus.JPG/440px-Echinococcus_granulosus.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Echinococcus_granulosus.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Echinococcus granulosus</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasites_of_humans" title="Parasites of humans">Parasites</a> can also infect the liver and activate the immune response, resulting in symptoms of acute hepatitis with increased serum <a href="/wiki/Immunoglobulin_E" title="Immunoglobulin E">IgE</a> (though chronic hepatitis is possible with chronic infections).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:31_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-:31-28">[28]</a></sup> Of the <a href="/wiki/Protozoa" title="Protozoa">protozoans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trypanosoma_cruzi" title="Trypanosoma cruzi">Trypanosoma cruzi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leishmaniasis" title="Leishmaniasis">Leishmania</a> species, and the <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a>-causing <a href="/wiki/Plasmodium" title="Plasmodium">Plasmodium</a> species all can cause liver inflammation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:31_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-:31-28">[28]</a></sup> Another protozoan, <a href="/wiki/Entamoeba_histolytica" title="Entamoeba histolytica">Entamoeba histolytica</a>, causes hepatitis with distinct liver abscesses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:31_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-:31-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>Of the worms, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cestode_infection" title="Cestode infection">cestode</a> <a href="/wiki/Echinococcus_granulosus" title="Echinococcus granulosus">Echinococcus granulosus</a>, also known as the dog tapeworm, infects the liver and forms characteristic hepatic <a href="/wiki/Echinococcosis" title="Echinococcosis">hydatid cysts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:31_28-3"><a href="#cite_note-:31-28">[28]</a></sup> The liver <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fluke_(parasite)" title="Fluke (parasite)">flukes</a> <a href="/wiki/Fasciola_hepatica" title="Fasciola hepatica">Fasciola hepatica</a> and <a href="/wiki/Clonorchis_sinensis" title="Clonorchis sinensis">Clonorchis sinensis</a> live in the bile ducts and cause progressive hepatitis and liver fibrosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:31_28-4"><a href="#cite_note-:31-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bacterial_hepatitis">Bacterial hepatitis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Bacterial hepatitis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Bacterial infection of the liver commonly results in <a href="/wiki/Pyogenic_liver_abscess" title="Pyogenic liver abscess">pyogenic liver abscesses</a>, acute hepatitis, or <a href="/wiki/Granuloma" title="Granuloma">granulomatous</a> (or chronic) liver disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:32_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-:32-29">[29]</a></sup> Pyogenic abscesses commonly involve <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enteric" title="Enteric">enteric</a> bacteria such as <i><a href="/wiki/Escherichia_coli" title="Escherichia coli">Escherichia coli</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Klebsiella_pneumoniae" title="Klebsiella pneumoniae">Klebsiella pneumoniae</a></i> and are composed of multiple bacteria up to 50% of the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:32_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-:32-29">[29]</a></sup> Acute hepatitis is caused by <i><a href="/wiki/Neisseria_meningitidis" title="Neisseria meningitidis">Neisseria meningitidis</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Neisseria_gonorrhoeae" title="Neisseria gonorrhoeae">Neisseria gonorrhoeae</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Bartonella_henselae" title="Bartonella henselae">Bartonella henselae</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Borrelia_burgdorferi" title="Borrelia burgdorferi">Borrelia burgdorferi</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Salmonella" title="Salmonella">salmonella</a> species, <a href="/wiki/Brucella" title="Brucella">brucella</a> species and <a href="/wiki/Campylobacter" title="Campylobacter">campylobacter</a> species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:32_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-:32-29">[29]</a></sup> Chronic or granulomatous hepatitis is seen with infection from <a href="/wiki/Mycobacterium" title="Mycobacterium">mycobacteria</a> species, <i><a href="/wiki/Tropheryma_whipplei" title="Tropheryma whipplei">Tropheryma whipplei</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Treponema_pallidum" title="Treponema pallidum">Treponema pallidum</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Coxiella_burnetii" title="Coxiella burnetii">Coxiella burnetii</a></i>, and <a href="/wiki/Rickettsia" title="Rickettsia">rickettsia</a> species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:32_29-3"><a href="#cite_note-:32-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metabolic">Metabolic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Metabolic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Alcoholic_hepatitis">Alcoholic hepatitis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Alcoholic hepatitis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Alcoholic_hepatitis" title="Alcoholic hepatitis">Alcoholic hepatitis</a></div>
<p>Excessive alcohol consumption is a significant cause of hepatitis and is the most common cause of cirrhosis in the U.S.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:33_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-:33-27">[27]</a></sup> Alcoholic hepatitis is within the spectrum of <a href="/wiki/Alcoholic_liver_disease" title="Alcoholic liver disease">alcoholic liver disease</a>. This ranges in order of severity and reversibility from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alcoholic_fatty_liver" title="Alcoholic fatty liver">alcoholic steatosis</a> (least severe, most reversible), <a href="/wiki/Alcoholic_hepatitis" title="Alcoholic hepatitis">alcoholic hepatitis</a>, cirrhosis, and liver cancer (most severe, least reversible).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:33_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-:33-27">[27]</a></sup> Hepatitis usually develops over years-long exposure to alcohol, occurring in 10 to 20% of alcoholics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:34_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-:34-30">[30]</a></sup> The most important risk factors for the development of alcoholic hepatitis are quantity and duration of alcohol intake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:34_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-:34-30">[30]</a></sup> Long-term alcohol intake in excess of 80 grams of alcohol a day in men and 40 grams a day in women is associated with development of alcoholic hepatitis (1 beer or 4 ounces of wine is equivalent to 12g of alcohol).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:33_27-3"><a href="#cite_note-:33-27">[27]</a></sup> Alcoholic hepatitis can vary from asymptomatic <a href="/wiki/Hepatomegaly" title="Hepatomegaly">hepatomegaly</a> (enlarged liver) to symptoms of acute or chronic hepatitis to liver failure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:33_27-4"><a href="#cite_note-:33-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Toxic_and_drug-induced_hepatitis">Toxic and drug-induced hepatitis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Toxic and drug-induced hepatitis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Many chemical agents, including medications, industrial toxins, and herbal and dietary supplements, can cause hepatitis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:35_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-:35-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:40_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-:40-32">[32]</a></sup> The spectrum of drug-induced liver injury varies from acute hepatitis to chronic hepatitis to acute liver failure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:35_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-:35-31">[31]</a></sup> Toxins and medications can cause liver injury through a variety of mechanisms, including direct <a href="/wiki/Cell_damage" title="Cell damage">cell damage</a>, disruption of cell metabolism, and causing structural changes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Some drugs such as <a href="/wiki/Paracetamol" title="Paracetamol">paracetamol</a> exhibit predictable dose-dependent liver damage while others such as <a href="/wiki/Isoniazid" title="Isoniazid">isoniazid</a> cause idiosyncratic and unpredictable reactions that vary among individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:35_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-:35-31">[31]</a></sup> There are wide variations in the mechanisms of liver injury and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latency_period" title="Latency period">latency period</a> from exposure to development of clinical illness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:33_27-5"><a href="#cite_note-:33-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many types of drugs can cause liver injury, including the <a href="/wiki/Analgesic" title="Analgesic">analgesic</a> paracetamol; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibiotic" title="Antibiotic">antibiotics</a> such as isoniazid, <a href="/wiki/Nitrofurantoin" title="Nitrofurantoin">nitrofurantoin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amoxicillin/clavulanic_acid" title="Amoxicillin/clavulanic acid">amoxicillin-clavulanate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Erythromycin" title="Erythromycin">erythromycin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Trimethoprim/sulfamethoxazole" title="Trimethoprim/sulfamethoxazole">trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole</a>; <a href="/wiki/Anticonvulsant" title="Anticonvulsant">anticonvulsants</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Valproate" title="Valproate">valproate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phenytoin" title="Phenytoin">phenytoin</a>; cholesterol-lowering <a href="/wiki/Statin" title="Statin">statins</a>; <a href="/wiki/Steroid" title="Steroid">steroids</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Oral_contraceptive_pill" title="Oral contraceptive pill">oral contraceptives</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anabolic_steroid" title="Anabolic steroid">anabolic steroids</a>; and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Highly_Active_Anti-Retroviral_Therapy" title="Highly Active Anti-Retroviral Therapy">highly active anti-retroviral therapy</a> used in the treatment of <a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS" title="HIV/AIDS">HIV/AIDS</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:33_27-6"><a href="#cite_note-:33-27">[27]</a></sup> Of these, amoxicillin-clavulanate is the most common cause of drug-induced liver injury, and <a href="/wiki/Paracetamol_toxicity" title="Paracetamol toxicity">paracetamol toxicity</a> the most common cause of acute liver failure in the United States and Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:35_31-3"><a href="#cite_note-:35-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Herb" title="Herb">Herbal remedies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dietary_supplement" title="Dietary supplement">dietary supplements</a> are another important cause of hepatitis; these are the most common causes of drug-induced hepatitis in Korea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> The United-States-based <a class="external text" href="http://www.dilin.org/" rel="nofollow">Drug Induced Liver Injury Network</a> linked more than 16% of cases of hepatotoxicity to herbal and dietary supplements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:36_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-:36-35">[35]</a></sup> In the United States, herbal and dietary supplements – unlike <a href="/wiki/Pharmaceutical_drug" title="Pharmaceutical drug">pharmaceutical drugs</a> – are unregulated by the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="Food and Drug Administration">Food and Drug Administration</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:36_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-:36-35">[35]</a></sup> However, the <a href="/wiki/National_Institutes_of_Health" title="National Institutes of Health">National Institutes of Health</a> maintains the <a class="external text" href="http://livertox.nih.gov/index.html" rel="nofollow">LiverTox</a> database for consumers to track all known prescription and non-prescription compounds associated with liver injury.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Exposure to other <a href="/wiki/Hepatotoxin" title="Hepatotoxin">hepatotoxins</a> can occur accidentally or intentionally through ingestion, inhalation, and skin absorption. The industrial toxin <a href="/wiki/Carbon_tetrachloride" title="Carbon tetrachloride">carbon tetrachloride</a> and the wild mushroom <a href="/wiki/Amanita_phalloides" title="Amanita phalloides">Amanita phalloides</a> are other known hepatotoxins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:35_31-4"><a href="#cite_note-:35-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:40_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-:40-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-alcoholic_fatty_liver_disease">Non-alcoholic fatty liver disease</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Non-alcoholic fatty liver disease">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Non-alcoholic_fatty_liver_disease" title="Non-alcoholic fatty liver disease">Non-alcoholic fatty liver disease</a></div>
<p>Non-alcoholic hepatitis is within the spectrum of non-alcoholic liver disease (NALD), which ranges in severity and reversibility from <a href="/wiki/Non-alcoholic_fatty_liver_disease" title="Non-alcoholic fatty liver disease">non-alcoholic fatty liver disease</a> (NAFLD) to non-alcoholic steatohepatitis (NASH) to cirrhosis to liver cancer, similar to the spectrum of alcoholic liver disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:37_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-:37-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>Non-alcoholic liver disease occurs in people with little or no history of alcohol use, and is instead strongly associated with <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_syndrome" title="Metabolic syndrome">metabolic syndrome</a>, obesity, <a href="/wiki/Insulin_resistance" title="Insulin resistance">insulin resistance</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diabetes_mellitus_type_2" title="Diabetes mellitus type 2">diabetes</a>, and hypertriglyceridemia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:33_27-7"><a href="#cite_note-:33-27">[27]</a></sup> Over time, non-alcoholic fatty liver disease can progress to non-alcoholic <a href="/wiki/Steatohepatitis" title="Steatohepatitis">steatohepatitis</a>, which additionally involves liver cell death, liver inflammation and possible fibrosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:33_27-8"><a href="#cite_note-:33-27">[27]</a></sup> Factors accelerating progression from NAFLD to NASH are obesity, older age, non-African American ethnicity, female gender, diabetes mellitus, hypertension, higher <a href="/wiki/Alanine_transaminase" title="Alanine transaminase">ALT</a> or <a href="/wiki/Aspartate_transaminase" title="Aspartate transaminase">AST</a> level, higher AST/ALT ratio, low platelet count, and an <a class="external text" href="http://www.nature.com/ijo/journal/v28/n10/fig_tab/0802734t1.html" rel="nofollow">ultrasound steatosis score</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:33_27-9"><a href="#cite_note-:33-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the early stages (as with NAFLD and early NASH), most patients are asymptomatic or have mild <a href="/wiki/Quadrant_(abdomen)" title="Quadrant (abdomen)">right upper quadrant</a> pain, and diagnosis is suspected on the basis of abnormal <a href="/wiki/Liver_function_tests" title="Liver function tests">liver function tests</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:33_27-10"><a href="#cite_note-:33-27">[27]</a></sup> As the disease progresses, symptoms typical of chronic hepatitis may develop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-National_Digestive_Diseases_Information_Clearinghouse_NDDIC_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-National_Digestive_Diseases_Information_Clearinghouse_NDDIC-38">[38]</a></sup> While imaging can show fatty liver, only <a href="/wiki/Liver_biopsy" title="Liver biopsy">liver biopsy</a> can demonstrate inflammation and fibrosis characteristic of NASH.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Masuoka_2013_106.E2.80.93122_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Masuoka_2013_106.E2.80.93122-39">[39]</a></sup> 9 to 25% of patients with NASH develop cirrhosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:33_27-11"><a href="#cite_note-:33-27">[27]</a></sup> NASH is recognized as the third most common cause of liver disease in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-National_Digestive_Diseases_Information_Clearinghouse_NDDIC_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-National_Digestive_Diseases_Information_Clearinghouse_NDDIC-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Autoimmune">Autoimmune</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Autoimmune">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Autoimmune_hepatitis" title="Autoimmune hepatitis">Autoimmune hepatitis</a></div>
<p>Autoimmune hepatitis is a chronic disease caused by an abnormal immune response against liver cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> The disease is thought to have a genetic predisposition as it is associated with certain <a href="/wiki/Human_leukocyte_antigen" title="Human leukocyte antigen">human leukocyte antigens</a> involved in the immune response.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> As in other autoimmune diseases, circulating <a href="/wiki/Autoantibody" title="Autoantibody">auto-antibodies</a> may be present and are helpful in diagnosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:41_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-:41-42">[42]</a></sup> Auto-antibodies found in patients with autoimmune hepatitis include the <a href="/wiki/Sensitivity_and_specificity" title="Sensitivity and specificity">sensitive but less specific</a> <a href="/wiki/Anti-nuclear_antibody" title="Anti-nuclear antibody">anti-nuclear antibody (ANA)</a>, smooth muscle antibody (SMA), and <a href="/wiki/Anti-neutrophil_cytoplasmic_antibody" title="Anti-neutrophil cytoplasmic antibody">atypical perinuclear antineutrophil cytoplasmic antibody (p-ANCA)</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:41_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-:41-42">[42]</a></sup> Other autoantibodies that are less common but more specific to autoimmune hepatitis are the antibodies against liver kidney microsome 1 (LKM1) and soluble liver antigen (SLA).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:41_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-:41-42">[42]</a></sup> Autoimmune hepatitis can also be triggered by drugs (such as <a href="/wiki/Nitrofurantoin" title="Nitrofurantoin">nitrofurantoin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydralazine" title="Hydralazine">hydralazine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Methyldopa" title="Methyldopa">methyldopa</a>), after liver transplant, or by viruses (such as hepatitis A, <a href="/wiki/Epstein%E2%80%93Barr_virus" title="Epstein–Barr virus">Epstein-Barr virus</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Measles" title="Measles">measles</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:33_27-12"><a href="#cite_note-:33-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>Autoimmune hepatitis can present anywhere within the spectrum from asymptomatic to acute or chronic hepatitis to fulminant liver failure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:33_27-13"><a href="#cite_note-:33-27">[27]</a></sup> Patients are asymptomatic 25–34% of the time, and the diagnosis is suspected on the basis of abnormal liver function tests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:41_42-3"><a href="#cite_note-:41-42">[42]</a></sup> Up to 40% of cases present with signs and symptoms of acute hepatitis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:33_27-14"><a href="#cite_note-:33-27">[27]</a></sup> As with other autoimmune diseases, autoimmune hepatitis usually affects young women (though it can affect patients of either sex of any age), and patients can exhibit classic signs and symptoms of autoimmunity such as fatigue, anemia, anorexia, <a href="/wiki/Amenorrhoea" title="Amenorrhoea">amenorrhea</a>, acne, arthritis, <a href="/wiki/Pleurisy" title="Pleurisy">pleurisy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thyroiditis" title="Thyroiditis">thyroiditis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ulcerative_colitis" title="Ulcerative colitis">ulcerative colitis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nephritis" title="Nephritis">nephritis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Maculopapular_rash" title="Maculopapular rash">maculopapular rash</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:33_27-15"><a href="#cite_note-:33-27">[27]</a></sup> Autoimmune hepatitis increases the risk for cirrhosis, and the risk for liver cancer is increased by about 1% for each year of the disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:33_27-16"><a href="#cite_note-:33-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many people with autoimmune hepatitis have other <a href="/wiki/Autoimmune_disease" title="Autoimmune disease">autoimmune diseases</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Autoimmune hepatitis is distinct from the other autoimmune diseases of the liver: <a href="/wiki/Primary_biliary_cirrhosis" title="Primary biliary cirrhosis">primary biliary cirrhosis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Primary_sclerosing_cholangitis" title="Primary sclerosing cholangitis">primary sclerosing cholangitis</a>. However, all of these diseases can lead to scarring, fibrosis, and cirrhosis of the liver.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:33_27-17"><a href="#cite_note-:33-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:41_42-4"><a href="#cite_note-:41-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetic">Genetic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Genetic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Genetic causes of hepatitis include <a href="/wiki/Alpha_1-antitrypsin_deficiency" title="Alpha 1-antitrypsin deficiency">alpha-1-antitrypsin deficiency</a>, <a href="/wiki/HFE_hereditary_haemochromatosis" title="HFE hereditary haemochromatosis">hemochromatosis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wilson%27s_disease" title="Wilson's disease">Wilson's disease</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:33_27-18"><a href="#cite_note-:33-27">[27]</a></sup> In alpha-1-antitrypsin deficiency, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Co-dominant_expression" title="Co-dominant expression">co-dominant</a> mutation in the gene for alpha-1-antitrypsin results in the abnormal accumulation of the protein within liver cells, leading to liver disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Hemochromatosis and Wilson's disease are both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autosomal_recessive" title="Autosomal recessive">autosomal recessive</a> diseases involving abnormal storage of minerals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:33_27-19"><a href="#cite_note-:33-27">[27]</a></sup> In hemochromatosis, excess amounts of iron accumulate in multiple body sites, including the liver, which can lead to cirrhosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:33_27-20"><a href="#cite_note-:33-27">[27]</a></sup> In Wilson's disease, excess amounts of copper accumulate in the liver and brain, causing cirrhosis and dementia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:33_27-21"><a href="#cite_note-:33-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>When the liver is involved, alpha-1-antitrypsin deficiency and Wilson's disease tend to present as hepatitis in the neonatal period or in childhood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:33_27-22"><a href="#cite_note-:33-27">[27]</a></sup> Hemochromatosis typically presents in adulthood, with the onset of clinical disease usually after age 50.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:33_27-23"><a href="#cite_note-:33-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ischemic_hepatitis">Ischemic hepatitis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Ischemic hepatitis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ischemic_hepatitis" title="Ischemic hepatitis">Ischemic hepatitis</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ischemic_hepatitis" title="Ischemic hepatitis">Ischemic hepatitis</a> (also known as shock liver) results from reduced blood flow to the liver as in shock, heart failure, or vascular insufficiency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:61_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-:61-45">[45]</a></sup> The condition is most often associated with <a href="/wiki/Heart_failure" title="Heart failure">heart failure</a> but can also be caused by <a href="/wiki/Shock_(circulatory)" title="Shock (circulatory)">shock</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sepsis" title="Sepsis">sepsis</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blood_testing" title="Blood testing">Blood testing</a> of a person with ischemic hepatitis will show very high levels of <a href="/wiki/Elevated_transaminases" title="Elevated transaminases">transaminase enzymes</a> (<a href="/wiki/Aspartate_transaminase" title="Aspartate transaminase">AST</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alanine_transaminase" title="Alanine transaminase">ALT</a>). The condition usually resolves if the underlying cause is treated successfully. Ischemic hepatitis rarely causes permanent liver damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Other">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Neonatal_hepatitis" title="Neonatal hepatitis">Neonatal hepatitis</a></div>
<p>Hepatitis can also occur in neonates and is attributable to a variety of causes, some of which are not typically seen in adults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:42_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-:42-47">[47]</a></sup> Congenital or perinatal infection with the hepatitis viruses, <a href="/wiki/Toxoplasma_gondii" title="Toxoplasma gondii">toxoplasma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rubella" title="Rubella">rubella</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cytomegalovirus" title="Cytomegalovirus">cytomegalovirus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Syphilis" title="Syphilis">syphilis</a> can cause neonatal hepatitis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:42_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-:42-47">[47]</a></sup> Structural abnormalities such as <a href="/wiki/Biliary_atresia" title="Biliary atresia">biliary atresia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Choledochal_cysts" title="Choledochal cysts">choledochal cysts</a> can lead to <a href="/wiki/Cholestasis" title="Cholestasis">cholestatic liver injury</a> leading to neonatal hepatitis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:42_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-:42-47">[47]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Inborn_error_of_metabolism" title="Inborn error of metabolism">Metabolic diseases</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Glycogen_storage_disease" title="Glycogen storage disease">glycogen storage disorders</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lysosomal_storage_disease" title="Lysosomal storage disease">lysosomal storage disorders</a> are also implicated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:42_47-3"><a href="#cite_note-:42-47">[47]</a></sup> Neonatal hepatitis can be <a href="/wiki/Idiopathy" title="Idiopathy">idiopathic</a>, and in such cases, biopsy often shows large multinucleated cells in the liver tissue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> This disease is termed giant cell hepatitis and may be associated with viral infection, autoimmune disorders, and drug toxicity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanism">Mechanism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Mechanism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The specific mechanism varies and depends on the underlying cause of the hepatitis. Generally, there is an initial insult that causes liver injury and activation of an inflammatory response, which can become chronic, leading to progressive <a href="/wiki/Fibrosis" title="Fibrosis">fibrosis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cirrhosis" title="Cirrhosis">cirrhosis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-21"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Viral_hepatitis_2">Viral hepatitis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Viral hepatitis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stages_of_Liver_disease_NIDDK_NIH.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="249" data-file-width="446" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Stages_of_Liver_disease_NIDDK_NIH.gif/240px-Stages_of_Liver_disease_NIDDK_NIH.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Stages_of_Liver_disease_NIDDK_NIH.gif/360px-Stages_of_Liver_disease_NIDDK_NIH.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/16/Stages_of_Liver_disease_NIDDK_NIH.gif 2x" width="240"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stages_of_Liver_disease_NIDDK_NIH.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Stages of liver disease</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The pathway by which hepatic viruses cause <a href="/wiki/Viral_hepatitis" title="Viral hepatitis">viral hepatitis</a> is best understood in the case of hepatitis B and C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-22"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup> The viruses do not directly cause <a href="/wiki/Apoptosis" title="Apoptosis">apoptosis</a> (cell death).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-23"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:14_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-:14-51">[51]</a></sup> Rather, infection of liver cells activates the <a href="/wiki/Innate_immune_system" title="Innate immune system">innate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_immune_system" title="Adaptive immune system">adaptive</a> arms of the <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">immune system</a> leading to an inflammatory response which causes cellular damage and death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-24"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:14_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-:14-51">[51]</a></sup> Depending on the strength of the immune response, the types of immune cells involved and the ability of the virus to evade the body's defense, infection can either lead to clearance (acute disease) or persistence (chronic disease) of the virus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-25"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup> The chronic presence of the virus within liver cells results in multiple waves of <a href="/wiki/Inflammation" title="Inflammation">inflammation</a>, injury and <a href="/wiki/Wound_healing" title="Wound healing">wound healing</a> that overtime lead to scarring or <a href="/wiki/Fibrosis" title="Fibrosis">fibrosis</a> and culminate in <a href="/wiki/Hepatocellular_carcinoma" title="Hepatocellular carcinoma">hepatocellular carcinoma</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:14_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-:14-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Individuals with an impaired immune response are at greater risk of developing chronic infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-26"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Natural_killer_cell" title="Natural killer cell">Natural killer cells</a> are the primary drivers of the initial innate response and create a <a href="/wiki/Cytokine" title="Cytokine">cytokine</a> environment that results in the recruitment of <a href="/wiki/T_helper_cell" title="T helper cell">CD4 T-helper</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cytotoxic_T_cell" title="Cytotoxic T cell">CD8 cytotoxic T-cells</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:15_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-:15-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:16_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-:16-54">[54]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Interferon_type_I" title="Interferon type I">Type I interferons</a> are the cytokines that drive the antiviral response.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:16_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-:16-54">[54]</a></sup> In chronic Hepatitis B and C, natural killer cell function is impaired.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:15_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-:15-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Steatohepatitis">Steatohepatitis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Steatohepatitis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Steatohepatitis" title="Steatohepatitis">Steatohepatitis</a> is seen in both alcoholic and non-alcoholic liver disease and is the culmination of a cascade of events that began with injury. In the case of <a href="/wiki/Non-alcoholic_fatty_liver_disease" title="Non-alcoholic fatty liver disease">non-alcoholic steatohepatitis</a>, this cascade is initiated by changes in metabolism associated with obesity, insulin resistance, and lipid dysregulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:56_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-:56-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:18_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-:18-56">[56]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Alcoholic_hepatitis" title="Alcoholic hepatitis">alcoholic hepatitis</a>, chronic excess alcohol use is the culprit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:50_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-:50-57">[57]</a></sup> Though the inciting event may differ, the progression of events is similar and begins with accumulation of free <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid" title="Fatty acid">fatty acids</a> (FFA) and their breakdown products in the liver cells in a process called <a href="/wiki/Steatosis" title="Steatosis">steatosis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:56_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-:56-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:18_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-:18-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:50_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-:50-57">[57]</a></sup> This initially reversible process overwhelms the <a href="/wiki/Hepatocyte" title="Hepatocyte">hepatocyte</a>'s ability to maintain lipid homeostasis leading to a toxic effect as fat molecules accumulate and are broken down in the setting of an <a href="/wiki/Cellular_stress_response" title="Cellular stress response">oxidative stress response</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:56_55-2"><a href="#cite_note-:56-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:18_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-:18-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:50_57-2"><a href="#cite_note-:50-57">[57]</a></sup> Overtime, this abnormal lipid deposition triggers the <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">immune system</a> via <a href="/wiki/Toll-like_receptor" title="Toll-like receptor">toll-like receptor 4</a> (TLR4) resulting in the production of inflammatory <a href="/wiki/Cytokine" title="Cytokine">cytokines</a> such as TNF that cause liver cell injury and death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:56_55-3"><a href="#cite_note-:56-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:18_56-3"><a href="#cite_note-:18-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:50_57-3"><a href="#cite_note-:50-57">[57]</a></sup> These events mark the transition to <a href="/wiki/Steatohepatitis" title="Steatohepatitis">steatohepatitis</a> and in the setting of chronic injury, <a href="/wiki/Fibrosis" title="Fibrosis">fibrosis</a> eventually develops setting up events that lead to cirrhosis and hepatocellular carcinoma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:56_55-4"><a href="#cite_note-:56-55">[55]</a></sup> Microscopically, changes that can be seen include steatosis with large and swollen hepatocytes (<a href="/wiki/Ballooning_degeneration" title="Ballooning degeneration">ballooning</a>), evidence of cellular injury and cell death (apoptosis, necrosis), evidence of inflammation in particular in <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">zone 3 of the liver</a>, variable degrees of fibrosis and <a href="/wiki/Mallory_body" title="Mallory body">Mallory bodies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:56_55-5"><a href="#cite_note-:56-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:58_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-:58-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diagnosis">Diagnosis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Diagnosis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

</tr>
<tr>
<td>Alpha-1-antitrypsin deficiency</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Hemochromatosis</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><i>Ischemic hepatitis (severe elevation up to thousands)</i></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td rowspan="2">AST</td>
<td>Alcoholic liver disease</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Cirrhosis</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Diagnosis of hepatitis is made on the basis of some or all of the following: a patient's signs and symptoms, medical history including sexual and substance use history, blood tests, <a href="/wiki/Medical_imaging" title="Medical imaging">imaging</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Liver_biopsy" title="Liver biopsy">liver biopsy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:33_27-24"><a href="#cite_note-:33-27">[27]</a></sup> In general, for viral hepatitis and other acute causes of hepatitis, the patient's blood tests and clinical picture are sufficient for diagnosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-27"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:33_27-25"><a href="#cite_note-:33-27">[27]</a></sup> For other causes of hepatitis, especially chronic causes, blood tests may not be useful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:33_27-26"><a href="#cite_note-:33-27">[27]</a></sup> In this case, liver biopsy is the <a href="/wiki/Gold_standard_(test)" title="Gold standard (test)">gold standard</a> for establishing the diagnosis as <a href="/wiki/Histopathology" title="Histopathology">histopathologic</a> analysis is able to reveal the precise extent and pattern of inflammation and <a href="/wiki/Fibrosis" title="Fibrosis">fibrosis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:33_27-27"><a href="#cite_note-:33-27">[27]</a></sup> However, liver biopsy is typically not the initial diagnostic test because it is invasive and is associated with a small but significant risk of bleeding that is increased in patients with liver injury and cirrhosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>Blood testing includes <a href="/wiki/Liver_function_tests" title="Liver function tests">liver enzymes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serology" title="Serology">serology</a> (i.e. for autoantibodies), <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid_test" title="Nucleic acid test">nucleic acid testing</a> (i.e. for hepatitis virus DNA/RNA), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blood_chemistry_study" title="Blood chemistry study">blood chemistry</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Complete_blood_count" title="Complete blood count">complete blood count</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:33_27-28"><a href="#cite_note-:33-27">[27]</a></sup> Characteristic patterns of liver enzyme abnormalities can point to certain causes or stages of hepatitis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Generally, <a href="/wiki/Aspartate_transaminase" title="Aspartate transaminase">AST</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alanine_transaminase" title="Alanine transaminase">ALT</a> are elevated in most cases of hepatitis regardless of whether the patient shows any symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:33_27-29"><a href="#cite_note-:33-27">[27]</a></sup> However, the degree of elevation (i.e. levels in the hundreds vs. in the thousands), the predominance for AST vs. ALT elevation, and the ratio between AST and ALT are informative of the diagnosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:33_27-30"><a href="#cite_note-:33-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Medical_ultrasound" title="Medical ultrasound">Ultrasound</a>, <a href="/wiki/CT_scan" title="CT scan">CT</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_resonance_imaging" title="Magnetic resonance imaging">MRI</a> can all identify steatosis (fatty changes) of the liver tissue and nodularity of the liver surface suggestive of cirrhosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> CT and especially MRI are able to provide a higher level of detail, allowing visualization and characterize such structures as vessels and tumors within the liver.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Unlike steatosis and cirrhosis, no imaging test is able to detect liver inflammation (i.e. hepatitis) or fibrosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:33_27-31"><a href="#cite_note-:33-27">[27]</a></sup> Liver biopsy is the only definitive diagnostic test that is able to assess inflammation and fibrosis of the liver.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:33_27-32"><a href="#cite_note-:33-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Viral_hepatitis_3">Viral hepatitis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Viral hepatitis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Viral_hepatitis" title="Viral hepatitis">Viral hepatitis</a></div>
<p>Viral hepatitis is primarily diagnosed through blood tests for levels of viral <a href="/wiki/Antigen" title="Antigen">antigens</a> (such as the <a href="/wiki/HBsAg" title="HBsAg">hepatitis B surface</a> or <a href="/wiki/HBcAg" title="HBcAg">core</a> antigen), anti-viral antibodies (such as the anti-hepatitis B surface antibody or anti-hepatitis A antibody), or viral DNA/RNA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-28"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:33_27-33"><a href="#cite_note-:33-27">[27]</a></sup> In early infection (i.e. within 1 week), <a href="/wiki/Immunoglobulin_M" title="Immunoglobulin M">IgM</a> antibodies are found in the blood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:33_27-34"><a href="#cite_note-:33-27">[27]</a></sup> In late infection and after recovery, <a href="/wiki/Immunoglobulin_G" title="Immunoglobulin G">IgG</a> antibodies are present and remain in the body for up to years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:33_27-35"><a href="#cite_note-:33-27">[27]</a></sup> Therefore, when a patient is positive for IgG antibody but negative for IgM antibody, he is considered <a href="/wiki/Immunity_(medical)" title="Immunity (medical)">immune</a> from the virus via either prior infection and recovery or prior vaccination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:33_27-36"><a href="#cite_note-:33-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the case of hepatitis B, blood tests exist for multiple virus antigens (which are different components of the <a href="/wiki/File:HBV.png" title="File:HBV.png">virion particle</a>) and antibodies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid26568915_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid26568915-66">[66]</a></sup> The combination of antigen and antibody positivity can provide information about the stage of infection (acute or chronic), the degree of viral replication, and the infectivity of the virus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid26568915_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid26568915-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alcoholic_versus_non-alcoholic">Alcoholic versus non-alcoholic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Alcoholic versus non-alcoholic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The most apparent distinguishing factor between <a href="/wiki/Alcoholic_hepatitis" title="Alcoholic hepatitis">alcoholic steatohepatitis</a> (ASH) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nonalcoholic_Steatohepatitis" title="Nonalcoholic Steatohepatitis">nonalcoholic steatohepatitis</a> (NASH) is a history of alcohol use or abuse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:46_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-:46-67">[67]</a></sup> Thus, in patients who have no or negligible alcohol use, the diagnosis is unlikely to be alcoholic hepatitis. However, in those who use alcohol, the diagnosis may just as likely be alcoholic or nonalcoholic hepatitis especially if there is concurrent obesity, diabetes, and metabolic syndrome. In this case, alcoholic and nonalcoholic hepatitis can be distinguished by the pattern of liver enzyme abnormalities; specifically, in alcoholic steatohepatitis AST&gt;ALT with ratio of AST:ALT&gt;2:1 while in nonalcoholic steatohepatitis ALT&gt;AST with ratio of ALT:AST&gt;1.5:1.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:46_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-:46-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>Of note, liver biopsy shows identical findings in patients with ASH and NASH, specifically, the presence of <a href="/wiki/Granulocyte" title="Granulocyte">polymorphonuclear</a> infiltration, hepatocyte <a href="/wiki/Necrosis" title="Necrosis">necrosis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Apoptosis" title="Apoptosis">apoptosis</a> in the form of <a href="/wiki/Ballooning_degeneration" title="Ballooning degeneration">ballooning degeneration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mallory_body" title="Mallory body">Mallory bodies</a>, and fibrosis around veins and sinuses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:33_27-37"><a href="#cite_note-:33-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Screening">Screening</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Screening">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Viral_hepatitis_4">Viral hepatitis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Viral hepatitis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The purpose of screening for viral hepatitis is to identify people infected with the disease as early as possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:21_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-:21-68">[68]</a></sup> This allows for early treatment, which can prevent disease progression, and decreases transmission to others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:21_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-:21-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:22_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-:22-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hepatitis_A">Hepatitis A</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Hepatitis A">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Hepatitis A causes an acute illness that does not progress to chronic liver disease. Therefore, the role of screening is to assess immune status in people who are at high risk of contracting the virus, as well as in people with known liver disease for whom hepatitis A infection could lead to liver failure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> People in these groups who are not already immune can receive the <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_A_vaccine" title="Hepatitis A vaccine">hepatitis A vaccine</a>.</p>
<p>Those at high risk and in need of screening include:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_Hep_A_Fact_Sheet_July_2015_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_Hep_A_Fact_Sheet_July_2015-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Voise_2011_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Voise_2011-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC_Adult_Vaccine_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDC_Adult_Vaccine-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>People with poor sanitary habits such as not washing hands after using the restroom or changing diapers</li>
<li>People who do not have access to clean water</li>
<li>People in close contact (either living with or having sexual contact) with someone who has hepatitis A</li>
<li>Illicit drug users</li>
<li>People with liver disease</li>
<li>People traveling to an area with endemic hepatitis A</li>
<li>Adoptive family members of a child from an area with endemic hepatitis A</li>
</ul>
<p>The presence of anti-hepatitis A <a href="/wiki/Immunoglobulin_G" title="Immunoglobulin G">IgG</a> in the blood indicates past infection with the virus or prior vaccination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hepatitis_B">Hepatitis B</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Hepatitis B">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
