<p>The <a href="/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention" title="Centers for Disease Control and Prevention">CDC</a>, <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">WHO</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Preventive_Services_Task_Force" title="United States Preventive Services Task Force">USPSTF</a>, and <a href="/wiki/American_Congress_of_Obstetricians_and_Gynecologists" title="American Congress of Obstetricians and Gynecologists">ACOG</a> recommend routine hepatitis B screening for certain high-risk populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:21_68-2"><a href="#cite_note-:21-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:22_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-:22-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:23_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-:23-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:24_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-:24-77">[77]</a></sup> Specifically, these populations include people who are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Born in countries where the prevalence of hepatitis B is high (defined as ≥2% of the population), whether or not they have been vaccinated<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:21_68-3"><a href="#cite_note-:21-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:22_69-2"><a href="#cite_note-:22-69">[69]</a></sup></li>
<li>Born in the United States whose parents are from countries where the prevalence of hepatitis B is very high (defined as ≥8% of the population), and who were not vaccinated<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:21_68-4"><a href="#cite_note-:21-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:22_69-3"><a href="#cite_note-:22-69">[69]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS" title="HIV/AIDS">HIV</a> positive<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:21_68-5"><a href="#cite_note-:21-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:22_69-4"><a href="#cite_note-:22-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:23_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-:23-76">[76]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intravenous_drug_users" title="Intravenous drug users">Intravenous drug users</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:21_68-6"><a href="#cite_note-:21-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:22_69-5"><a href="#cite_note-:22-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:23_76-2"><a href="#cite_note-:23-76">[76]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Men_who_have_sex_with_men" title="Men who have sex with men">Men who have sex with men</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:21_68-7"><a href="#cite_note-:21-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:22_69-6"><a href="#cite_note-:22-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:23_76-3"><a href="#cite_note-:23-76">[76]</a></sup></li>
<li>In close contact with (i.e. live and/or have sex with) people known to have hepatitis B<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:21_68-8"><a href="#cite_note-:21-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:22_69-7"><a href="#cite_note-:22-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:23_76-4"><a href="#cite_note-:23-76">[76]</a></sup></li>
<li>Pregnant<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:21_68-9"><a href="#cite_note-:21-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:22_69-8"><a href="#cite_note-:22-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:24_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-:24-77">[77]</a></sup></li>
<li>Beginning <a href="/wiki/Immunosuppressive_drug" title="Immunosuppressive drug">immunosuppressive</a> and/or <a href="/wiki/Chemotherapy" title="Chemotherapy">cytotoxic therapy</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:21_68-10"><a href="#cite_note-:21-68">[68]</a></sup></li>
<li>Found to have elevated <a href="/wiki/Liver_function_tests" title="Liver function tests">liver enzymes</a> without a known cause<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:21_68-11"><a href="#cite_note-:21-68">[68]</a></sup></li>
<li>Blood, organ, and/or tissue donors<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:23_76-5"><a href="#cite_note-:23-76">[76]</a></sup></li>
<li>Incarcerated<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:23_76-6"><a href="#cite_note-:23-76">[76]</a></sup></li>
<li>On <a href="/wiki/Hemodialysis" title="Hemodialysis">hemodialysis</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:21_68-12"><a href="#cite_note-:21-68">[68]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>Screening consists of a blood test that detects hepatitis B surface antigen (<a href="/wiki/HBsAg" title="HBsAg">HBsAg</a>). If HBsAg is present, a second test – usually done on the same blood sample – that detects the antibody for the hepatitis B core antigen (anti-<a href="/wiki/HBcAg" title="HBcAg">HBcAg</a>) can differentiate between acute and chronic infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:21_68-13"><a href="#cite_note-:21-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> People who are high-risk whose blood tests negative for HBsAg can receive the <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_B_vaccine" title="Hepatitis B vaccine">hepatitis B vaccine</a> to prevent future infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:21_68-14"><a href="#cite_note-:21-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:22_69-9"><a href="#cite_note-:22-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:23_76-7"><a href="#cite_note-:23-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:24_77-2"><a href="#cite_note-:24-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hepatitis_C">Hepatitis C</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Hepatitis C">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention" title="Centers for Disease Control and Prevention">CDC</a>, <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">WHO</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Preventive_Services_Task_Force" title="United States Preventive Services Task Force">USPSTF</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AASLD" title="AASLD">AASLD</a>, and <a href="/wiki/American_Congress_of_Obstetricians_and_Gynecologists" title="American Congress of Obstetricians and Gynecologists">ACOG</a> recommend screening people at high risk for hepatitis C infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:24_77-3"><a href="#cite_note-:24-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:53_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-:53-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:10_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-:10-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:54_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-:54-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:55_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-:55-82">[82]</a></sup> These populations include people who are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Intravenous drug users (past or current)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:53_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-:53-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:10_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-:10-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:54_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-:54-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:55_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-:55-82">[82]</a></sup></li>
<li>Intranasal illicit drug users<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:53_79-2"><a href="#cite_note-:53-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:10_80-2"><a href="#cite_note-:10-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:54_81-2"><a href="#cite_note-:54-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:55_82-2"><a href="#cite_note-:55-82">[82]</a></sup></li>
<li>HIV-positive<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:53_79-3"><a href="#cite_note-:53-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:10_80-3"><a href="#cite_note-:10-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:54_81-3"><a href="#cite_note-:54-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:55_82-3"><a href="#cite_note-:55-82">[82]</a></sup></li>
<li>Men who have sex with men<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:55_82-4"><a href="#cite_note-:55-82">[82]</a></sup></li>
<li>Incarcerated, or who have been in the past<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:53_79-4"><a href="#cite_note-:53-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:10_80-4"><a href="#cite_note-:10-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:54_81-4"><a href="#cite_note-:54-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:55_82-5"><a href="#cite_note-:55-82">[82]</a></sup></li>
<li>On long-term hemodialysis, or who have been in the past<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:53_79-5"><a href="#cite_note-:53-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:10_80-5"><a href="#cite_note-:10-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:54_81-5"><a href="#cite_note-:54-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:55_82-6"><a href="#cite_note-:55-82">[82]</a></sup></li>
<li>Recipients of tattoos in an "unregulated setting"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:54_81-6"><a href="#cite_note-:54-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:55_82-7"><a href="#cite_note-:55-82">[82]</a></sup></li>
<li>Recipients of blood products or organs prior to 1992 in the United States<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:53_79-6"><a href="#cite_note-:53-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:54_81-7"><a href="#cite_note-:54-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:55_82-8"><a href="#cite_note-:55-82">[82]</a></sup></li>
<li>Adults in the United States born between 1945-1965<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:54_81-8"><a href="#cite_note-:54-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:55_82-9"><a href="#cite_note-:55-82">[82]</a></sup></li>
<li>Born to HCV-positive mothers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:55_82-10"><a href="#cite_note-:55-82">[82]</a></sup></li>
<li>Pregnant, and engaging in high-risk behaviors<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:24_77-4"><a href="#cite_note-:24-77">[77]</a></sup></li>
<li>Workers in a healthcare setting who have had a needlestick injury<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:55_82-11"><a href="#cite_note-:55-82">[82]</a></sup></li>
<li>Blood and/or organ donors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:55_82-12"><a href="#cite_note-:55-82">[82]</a></sup></li>
<li>Sex workers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:10_80-6"><a href="#cite_note-:10-80">[80]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>For people in the groups above whose exposure is ongoing, screening should be "periodic," though according to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USPSTF" title="USPSTF">USPSTF</a>, research has not defined the optimal screening interval.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:54_81-9"><a href="#cite_note-:54-81">[81]</a></sup> The AASLD recommends screening men who have sex with men who are HIV-positive annually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:55_82-13"><a href="#cite_note-:55-82">[82]</a></sup> People born in the US between 1945-1965 should be screened once (unless they have other exposure risks).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:53_79-7"><a href="#cite_note-:53-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:54_81-10"><a href="#cite_note-:54-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:55_82-14"><a href="#cite_note-:55-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p>Screening consists of a blood test that detects anti-hepatitis C virus antibody. If anti-hepatitis C virus antibody is present, a confirmatory test to detect HCV RNA indicates chronic disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:10_80-7"><a href="#cite_note-:10-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:55_82-15"><a href="#cite_note-:55-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevention">Prevention</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Prevention">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vaccines">Vaccines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Vaccines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:0-18yrs-child-combined-schedule.pdf"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1275" data-file-width="1650" height="270" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/0-18yrs-child-combined-schedule.pdf/page1-350px-0-18yrs-child-combined-schedule.pdf.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/0-18yrs-child-combined-schedule.pdf/page1-525px-0-18yrs-child-combined-schedule.pdf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/0-18yrs-child-combined-schedule.pdf/page1-700px-0-18yrs-child-combined-schedule.pdf.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:0-18yrs-child-combined-schedule.pdf" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Recommended immunization schedules in the United States for ages 0 through 18 years as of 2016</div>
</div>
</div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hepatitis_A_2">Hepatitis A</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Hepatitis A">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_A_vaccine" title="Hepatitis A vaccine">Hepatitis A vaccine</a></div>
<p>The CDC recommends the <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_A_vaccine" title="Hepatitis A vaccine">hepatitis A vaccine</a> for all children beginning at age one, as well as for those who have not been previously immunized and are at high risk for contracting the disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_Hep_A_Fact_Sheet_July_2015_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_Hep_A_Fact_Sheet_July_2015-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Voise_2011_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-Voise_2011-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>For children 12 months of age or older, the vaccination is given as a shot into the muscle in two doses 6–18 months apart and should be started before the age 24 months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC_Birth-18Y_Immunizations_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDC_Birth-18Y_Immunizations-83">[83]</a></sup>  The dosing is slightly different for adults depending on the type of the vaccine.  If the vaccine is for hepatitis A only, two doses are given 6–18 months apart depending on the manufacturer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC_Adult_Vaccine_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-CDC_Adult_Vaccine-74">[74]</a></sup>  If the vaccine is <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_A_vaccine" title="Hepatitis A vaccine">combined hepatitis A and hepatitis B</a>, up to 4 doses may be required.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC_Adult_Vaccine_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-CDC_Adult_Vaccine-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hepatitis_B_2">Hepatitis B</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Hepatitis B">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_B_vaccine" title="Hepatitis B vaccine">Hepatitis B vaccine</a></div>
<p>The CDC recommends the routine vaccination of all children under the age of 19 with the <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_B_vaccine" title="Hepatitis B vaccine">hepatitis B vaccine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC_Update_1999_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDC_Update_1999-84">[84]</a></sup> They also recommend it for those who desire it or are at high risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Voise_2011_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-Voise_2011-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>Routine vaccination for hepatitis B starts with the first dose administered as  a shot into the muscle before the newborn is discharged from the hospital. An additional two doses should be administered before the child is 18 months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC_Birth-18Y_Immunizations_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-CDC_Birth-18Y_Immunizations-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>For babies born to a mother with hepatitis B surface antigen positivity, the first dose is unique – in addition to the vaccine, the hepatitis immune globulin should also be administered, both within 12 hours of birth.  These newborns should also be regularly tested for infection for at least the first year of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC_Birth-18Y_Immunizations_83-2"><a href="#cite_note-CDC_Birth-18Y_Immunizations-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>There is also a combination formulation that includes <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_A_vaccine" title="Hepatitis A vaccine">both hepatitis A and B vaccines</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_2">Other</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Other">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>There are currently no vaccines available in the United States for hepatitis C or E.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:10_80-8"><a href="#cite_note-:10-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:13_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-:13-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:11_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-:11-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Behavioral_changes">Behavioral changes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Behavioral changes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hepatitis_A_3">Hepatitis A</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Hepatitis A">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Because hepatitis A is transmitted primarily through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fecal-oral_route" title="Fecal-oral route">oral-fecal route</a>, the mainstay of prevention aside from vaccination is good hygiene, access to clean water and proper handling of sewage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Voise_2011_73-3"><a href="#cite_note-Voise_2011-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hepatitis_B_and_C">Hepatitis B and C</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Hepatitis B and C">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>As hepatitis B and C are transmitted through blood and multiple <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bodily_fluids" title="Bodily fluids">bodily fluids</a>, prevention is aimed at screening blood prior to <a href="/wiki/Blood_transfusion" title="Blood transfusion">transfusion</a>, abstaining from the use of injection drugs, safe needle and sharps practices in healthcare settings, and safe sex practices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-:8-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:10_80-9"><a href="#cite_note-:10-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hepatitis_D">Hepatitis D</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Hepatitis D">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The hepatitis D virus requires that a person first be infected with hepatitis B virus, so prevention efforts should focus on limiting the spread of hepatitis B. In people who have chronic hepatitis B infection and are at risk for <a href="/wiki/Superinfection" title="Superinfection">superinfection</a> with the hepatitis D virus, the preventive strategies are the same as for hepatitis B.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:11_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-:11-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hepatitis_E">Hepatitis E</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Hepatitis E">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Hepatitis E is spread primarily through the oral-fecal route but may also be spread by blood and from mother to fetus. The mainstay of hepatitis E prevention is similar to that for hepatitis A (namely, good hygiene and clean water practices).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:13_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-:13-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Alcoholic_hepatitis_2">Alcoholic hepatitis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Alcoholic hepatitis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>As excessive alcohol consumption can lead to hepatitis and cirrhosis, the following are maximal recommendations for alcohol consumption:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Women – ≤ 3 drinks on any given day and ≤ 7drinks per week</li>
<li>Men – ≤ 4 drinks on any given day and ≤ 14 drinks per week</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Successes">Successes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Successes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hepatitis_A_4">Hepatitis A</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Hepatitis A">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In the United States, universal immunization has led to a two-thirds decrease in hospital admissions and medical expenses due to hepatitis A.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hepatitis_B_3">Hepatitis B</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Hepatitis B">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In the United States new cases of hepatitis B decreased 75% from 1990–2004.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>  The group that saw the greatest decrease was children and adolescents, likely reflecting the implementation of the 1999 guidelines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-:7-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hepatitis_C_2">Hepatitis C</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Hepatitis C">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Hepatitis C infections each year had been declining since the 1980s, but began to increase again in 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> The data are unclear as to whether the decline can be attributed to <a href="/wiki/Needle_exchange_programme" title="Needle exchange programme">needle exchange programmes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Alcoholic_hepatitis_3">Alcoholic hepatitis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Alcoholic hepatitis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Because people with alcoholic hepatitis may have no symptoms, it can be difficult to diagnose and the number of people with the disease is probably higher than many estimates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Programs such as <a href="/wiki/Alcoholics_Anonymous" title="Alcoholics Anonymous">Alcoholics Anonymous</a> have been successful in decreasing death due to <a href="/wiki/Cirrhosis" title="Cirrhosis">cirrhosis</a>, but it is difficult to evaluate their success in decreasing the incidence of alcoholic hepatitis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Treatment">Treatment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Treatment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Treatment of hepatitis varies based on the form (acute versus chronic), severity of disease, and cause. </p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hepatitis_A_5">Hepatitis A</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Hepatitis A">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_A" title="Hepatitis A">Hepatitis A</a></div>
<p>Hepatitis A generally does not progress to a chronic state and rarely requires hospitalization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-29"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_Hep_A_Fact_Sheet_July_2015_72-2"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_Hep_A_Fact_Sheet_July_2015-72">[72]</a></sup> Treatment is supportive and includes such measures as providing intravenous (IV) hydration and maintaining adequate nutrition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-30"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_Hep_A_Fact_Sheet_July_2015_72-3"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_Hep_A_Fact_Sheet_July_2015-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>Rarely, people with the hepatitis A virus can rapidly develop liver failure, termed <i>fulminant hepatic failure</i>, especially the elderly and those who had a pre-existing liver disease, especially hepatitis C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-31"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_Hep_A_Fact_Sheet_July_2015_72-4"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_Hep_A_Fact_Sheet_July_2015-72">[72]</a></sup> Mortality risk factors include greater age and chronic hepatitis C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-32"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup> In these cases, more aggressive supportive therapy and liver transplant may be necessary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-33"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hepatitis_B_4">Hepatitis B</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Hepatitis B">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_B" title="Hepatitis B">Hepatitis B</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Acute">Acute</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Acute">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In healthy patients, 95–99% recover with no long-lasting effects, and antiviral treatment is not warranted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-34"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup> Age and comorbid conditions can result in a more prolonged and severe illness. Certain patients warrant hospitalization, especially those who present with clinical signs of ascites, peripheral edema, and hepatic encephalopathy, and laboratory signs of <a href="/wiki/Hypoglycemia" title="Hypoglycemia">hypoglycemia</a>, prolonged <a href="/wiki/Prothrombin_time" title="Prothrombin time">prothrombin time</a>, low serum <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Albumin_human" title="Albumin human">albumin</a>, and very high serum <a href="/wiki/Bilirubin" title="Bilirubin">blirubin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-35"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>In these rare, more severe acute cases, patients have been successfully treated with antiviral therapy similar to that used in cases of chronic hepatitis B, with nucleoside analogues such as <a href="/wiki/Entecavir" title="Entecavir">entecavir</a> or <a href="/wiki/Tenofovir_disoproxil" title="Tenofovir disoproxil">tenofovir</a>. As there is a dearth of clinical trial data and the drugs used to treat are prone to developing <a href="/wiki/Drug_resistance" title="Drug resistance">resistance</a>, experts recommend reserving treatment for severe acute cases, not mild to moderate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-36"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Chronic">Chronic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Chronic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Chronic hepatitis B management aims to control viral replication, which is correlated with progression of disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-6"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup> There have been 7 drug treatments approved to date in the United States:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-7"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Injectable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interferon_alpha" title="Interferon alpha">interferon alpha</a> was the first therapy approved for chronic hepatitis B.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-8"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup> It has several side effects, most of which are reversible with removal of therapy, but it has been supplanted by newer treatments for this indication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-9"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup> These include long-acting interferon bound to <a href="/wiki/Polyethylene_glycol" title="Polyethylene glycol">polyethylene glycol</a> (pegylated interferon) and the oral nucleoside analogues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-10"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pegylated_interferon" title="Pegylated interferon">Pegylated interferon</a> (PEG IFN) is dosed just once a week as a subcutaneous injection and is both more convenient and effective than standard interferon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-11"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup> Although it does not develop resistance as do many of the oral antivirals, it is poorly tolerated and requires close monitoring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-12"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup> PEG IFN is estimated to cost about $18,000 per year in the United States, compared to $2,500-8,700 for the oral medications; however, its treatment duration is 48 weeks as opposed to the oral antivirals, which require indefinite treatment for most patients (minimum 1 year).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-13"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup> PEG IFN is not effective in patients with high levels of viral activity and cannot be used in immunosuppressed patients or those with cirrhosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-14"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lamivudine" title="Lamivudine">Lamivudine</a> was the first approved oral nucleoside analogue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-15"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup> While effective and potent, lamivudine has been replaced by newer, more potent treatments in the Western world and is no longer recommended as first-line treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-16"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup> However, it is still used in areas where newer agents either have not been approved or are too costly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-17"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup> Generally, the course of treatment is a minimum of one year with a minimum of six additional months of "consolidation therapy."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-18"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup> Based on viral response, longer therapy may be required, and certain patients require indefinite long-term therapy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-19"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup> Due to a less robust response in Asian patients, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Consolidation_therapy" title="Consolidation therapy">consolidation therapy</a> is recommended to be extended to at least a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-20"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup> All patients should be monitored for viral reactivation, which if identified, requires restarting treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-21"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup> Lamivudine is generally safe and well-tolerated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-22"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup> Many patients develop resistance, which is correlated with longer treatment duration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-23"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup> If this occurs, an additional antiviral is added.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-24"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup> Lamivudine as a single treatment is contraindicated in patients coinfected with HIV, as resistance develops rapidly, but it can be used as part of a multidrug regimen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-25"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adefovir_dipivoxil" title="Adefovir dipivoxil">Adefovir dipivoxil</a>, a nucleotide analogue, has been used to supplement lamivudine in patients who develop resistance, but is no longer recommended as first-line therapy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-26"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Entecavir" title="Entecavir">Entecavir</a> is safe, well tolerated, less prone to developing resistance, and the most potent of the existing hepatitis B antivirals; it is thus a first-line treatment choice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-27"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup> It is not recommended for lamivudine-resistant patients or as monotherapy in patients who are HIV positive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-28"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Telbivudine" title="Telbivudine">Telbivudine</a> is effective but not recommended as first-line treatment; as compared to entecavir, it is both less potent and more resistance prone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-29"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tenofovir_disoproxil" title="Tenofovir disoproxil">Tenofovir</a> is a nucleotide analogue and an antiretroviral drug that is also used to treat HIV infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-30"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup> It is preferred to adefovir both in lamivudine-resistant patients and as initial treatment since it is both more potent and less likely to develop resistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-31"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>First-line treatments currently used include PEG IFN, entecavir, and tenofovir, subject to patient and physician preference.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-32"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup> Treatment initiation is guided by recommendations issued by The American Association for the Study of Liver Diseases (AASLD) and the European Association for the Study of the Liver (EASL) and is based on detectable viral levels, <a href="/wiki/HBeAg" title="HBeAg">HBeAg</a> positive or negative status, <a href="/wiki/Alanine_transaminase" title="Alanine transaminase">ALT</a> levels, and in certain cases, family history of HCC and liver biopsy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-33"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup> In patients with compensated cirrhosis, treatment is recommended regardless of HBeAg status or ALT level, but recommendations differ regarding HBV DNA levels; AASLD recommends treating at DNA levels detectable above 2x10<sup>3</sup> IU/mL; EASL and WHO recommend treating when HBV DNA levels are detectable at any level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-34"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:23_76-8"><a href="#cite_note-:23-76">[76]</a></sup> In patients with decompensated cirrhosis, treatment and evaluation for liver transplantation are recommended in all cases if HBV DNA is detectable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-35"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:23_76-9"><a href="#cite_note-:23-76">[76]</a></sup> Currently, multidrug treatment is not recommended in treatment of chronic HBV as it is no more effective in the long term than individual treatment with entecavir or tenofovir.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-36"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hepatitis_C_3">Hepatitis C</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Hepatitis C">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_C" title="Hepatitis C">Hepatitis C</a></div>
<p>In contrast to hepatitis A and B, progression to chronic hepatitis C is much more common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-37"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup> The ultimate goal of hepatitis C treatment is prevention of hepatocellular carcinoma (HCC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:17_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-:17-95">[95]</a></sup> The best way to reduce the long-term risk of HCC is to achieve sustained virological response (SVR).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:17_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-:17-95">[95]</a></sup> SVR is defined as an undetectable viral load at 12 weeks after treatment completion and indicates a cure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:59_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-:59-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:60_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-:60-97">[97]</a></sup> Currently available treatments include indirect and direct acting antiviral drugs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:59_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-:59-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:60_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-:60-97">[97]</a></sup> The indirect acting antivirals include pegylated interferon (PEG IFN) and ribavirin (RBV), which in combination have historically been the basis of therapy for HCV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:59_96-2"><a href="#cite_note-:59-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:60_97-2"><a href="#cite_note-:60-97">[97]</a></sup> Duration of and response to these treatments varies based on genotype.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:59_96-3"><a href="#cite_note-:59-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:60_97-3"><a href="#cite_note-:60-97">[97]</a></sup> These agents are poorly tolerated but are still used in some resource-poor areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:59_96-4"><a href="#cite_note-:59-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:60_97-4"><a href="#cite_note-:60-97">[97]</a></sup> In high-resource countries, they have been supplanted by direct acting antiviral agents, which first appeared in 2011; these agents target proteins responsible for viral replication and include the following three classes:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:59_96-5"><a href="#cite_note-:59-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:60_97-5"><a href="#cite_note-:60-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>NS3/4A protease inhibitors, including telaprevir, boceprevir, simeprevir, and others</li>
<li>NS5A inhibitors, including ledipasvir, daclatasvir, and others</li>
<li>NS5B polymerase inhibitors, including sofosbuvir, dasabuvir, and others</li>
</ul>
<p>These drugs are used in various combinations, sometimes combined with ribavirin, based on the patient's genotype (delineated as genotypes 1-6).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:60_97-6"><a href="#cite_note-:60-97">[97]</a></sup> Genotype 1 (GT1), which is the most prevalent genotype in the United States and around the world, can now be cured with a direct acting antiviral regimen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:60_97-7"><a href="#cite_note-:60-97">[97]</a></sup> First-line therapy for GT1 is a combination of sofosbuvir and ledipasvir (SOF/LDV) for 12 weeks for most patients, including those with advanced fibrosis or cirrhosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:60_97-8"><a href="#cite_note-:60-97">[97]</a></sup> Certain patients with early disease need only 8 weeks of treatment while those with advanced fibrosis or cirrhosis who have not responded to prior treatment require 24 weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:60_97-9"><a href="#cite_note-:60-97">[97]</a></sup> Cost remains a major factor limiting access to these drugs, particularly in low-resource nations; the cost of the 12-week GT1 regimen (SOF/LDV) has been estimated at US$94,500.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:59_96-6"><a href="#cite_note-:59-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>The American Association for the Study of Liver Diseases and the Infectious Diseases Society of America (AASLD-IDSA) recommend antiviral treatment for all patients with chronic hepatitis C infection except for those with additional chronic medical conditions that limit their life expectancy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:55_82-16"><a href="#cite_note-:55-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hepatitis_D_2">Hepatitis D</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Hepatitis D">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_D" title="Hepatitis D">Hepatitis D</a></div>
<p>Hepatitis D is difficult to treat, and effective treatments are lacking. Interferon alpha has proven effective at inhibiting viral activity but only on a temporary basis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:48_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-:48-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hepatitis_E_2">Hepatitis E</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Hepatitis E">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_E" title="Hepatitis E">Hepatitis E</a></div>
<p>Similar to hepatitis A, treatment of hepatitis E is supportive and includes rest and ensuring adequate nutrition and hydration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:49_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-:49-99">[99]</a></sup> Hospitalization may be required for particularly severe cases or for pregnant women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:49_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-:49-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alcoholic_hepatitis_4">Alcoholic hepatitis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Alcoholic hepatitis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>First-line treatment of alcoholic hepatitis is treatment of alcoholism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:34_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-:34-30">[30]</a></sup> For those who abstain completely from alcohol, reversal of liver disease and a longer life are possible; patients at every disease stage have been shown to benefit by prevention of additional liver injury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:34_30-3"><a href="#cite_note-:34-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:50_57-4"><a href="#cite_note-:50-57">[57]</a></sup> In addition to referral to psychotherapy and other treatment programs, treatment should include nutritional and psychosocial evaluation and treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:34_30-4"><a href="#cite_note-:34-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:50_57-5"><a href="#cite_note-:50-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:51_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-:51-100">[100]</a></sup> Patients should also be treated appropriately for related signs and symptoms, such as ascites, hepatic encephalopathy, and infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:50_57-6"><a href="#cite_note-:50-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>Severe alcoholic hepatitis has a poor prognosis and is notoriously difficult to treat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:34_30-5"><a href="#cite_note-:34-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:50_57-7"><a href="#cite_note-:50-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:51_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-:51-100">[100]</a></sup> Without any treatment, 20-50% of patients may die within a month, but evidence shows treatment may extend life beyond one month (i.e., reduce short-term mortality).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:34_30-6"><a href="#cite_note-:34-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:51_100-2"><a href="#cite_note-:51-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:52_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-:52-101">[101]</a></sup> Available treatment options include <a href="/wiki/Pentoxifylline" title="Pentoxifylline">pentoxifylline</a> (PTX), which is a nonspecific <a href="/wiki/TNF_inhibitor" title="TNF inhibitor">TNF inhibitor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Corticosteroid" title="Corticosteroid">corticosteroids</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Prednisone" title="Prednisone">prednisone</a> or <a href="/wiki/Prednisolone" title="Prednisolone">prednisolone</a> (CS), corticosteroids with <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/N-Acetylcysteine" title="N-Acetylcysteine">N</a></i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/N-Acetylcysteine" title="N-Acetylcysteine">-acetylcysteine</a> (CS with NAC), and corticosteroids with pentoxifylline (CS with PTX).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:51_100-3"><a href="#cite_note-:51-100">[100]</a></sup> Data suggest that CS alone or CS with NAC are most effective at reducing short-term mortality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:51_100-4"><a href="#cite_note-:51-100">[100]</a></sup> Unfortunately, corticosteroids are contraindicated in some patients, such as those who have active gastrointestinal bleeding, infection, kidney failure, or pancreatitis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:34_30-7"><a href="#cite_note-:34-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:50_57-8"><a href="#cite_note-:50-57">[57]</a></sup> In these cases PTX may be considered on a case by case basis in lieu of CS; some evidence shows PTX is better than no treatment at all and may be comparable to CS while other data show no evidence of benefit over placebo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:51_100-5"><a href="#cite_note-:51-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:52_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-:52-101">[101]</a></sup> Unfortunately, there are currently no drug treatments that decrease these patients’ risk of dying in the longer term, at 3–12 months and beyond.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:51_100-6"><a href="#cite_note-:51-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>Weak evidence suggests <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milk_Thistle" title="Milk Thistle">milk thistle</a> extracts may improve survival in alcoholic liver disease and improve certain liver tests (serum bilirubin and <a href="/wiki/Gamma-glutamyl_transpeptidase" title="Gamma-glutamyl transpeptidase">GGT</a>) without causing side effects, but a firm recommendation cannot be made for or against milk thistle without further study.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> </p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recommendations">Recommendations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: Recommendations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many people with hepatitis will prefer bed rest, though it is not necessary to avoid all physical activity while recovering.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-38"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup> A high-calorie diet is recommended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-39"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup> Many people develop nausea and cannot tolerate food later in the day, so the bulk of intake may be concentrated in the earlier part of the day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-40"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup> In the acute phase of the disease, intravenous feeding may be needed if patients cannot tolerate food and have poor oral intake subsequent to nausea and vomiting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-41"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>People with hepatitis should avoid taking drugs metabolized by the liver.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-42"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Glucocorticoid" title="Glucocorticoid">Glucocorticoids</a> are not recommended as a treatment option for acute viral hepatitis and may even cause harm, such as development of chronic hepatitis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-43"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Universal_precautions" title="Universal precautions">Universal precautions</a> should be observed, but isolation is not strictly necessary; isolation may be needed in cases of fecal incontinence in hepatitis A and E and uncontrolled bleeding in hepatitis B and C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-44"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prognosis">Prognosis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=55" title="Edit section: Prognosis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Acute_hepatitis_2">Acute hepatitis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=56" title="Edit section: Acute hepatitis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Nearly all patients with hepatitis A infections recover completely without complications if they were healthy prior to the infection. Similarly, acute hepatitis B infections have a favorable course towards complete recovery in 95–99% of patients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-45"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup> However, certain factors may portend a poorer outcome, such as co-morbid medical conditions or initial presenting symptoms of ascites, edema, or encephalopathy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-46"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup> Overall, the mortality rate for acute hepatitis is low: ~0.1% in total for cases of hepatitis A and B, but rates can be higher in certain populations (super infection with both hepatitis B and D, pregnant women, etc.).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-47"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>In contrast to hepatitis A &amp; B, hepatitis C carries a much higher risk of progressing to chronic hepatitis, approaching 85–90%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Cirrhosis has been reported to develop in 20–50% of patients with chronic hepatitis C.</p>
<p>Other rare complications of acute hepatitis include <a href="/wiki/Pancreatitis" title="Pancreatitis">pancreatitis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aplastic_anemia" title="Aplastic anemia">aplastic anemia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peripheral_neuropathy" title="Peripheral neuropathy">peripheral neuropathy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Myocarditis" title="Myocarditis">myocarditis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-48"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fulminant_hepatitis_2">Fulminant hepatitis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=57" title="Edit section: Fulminant hepatitis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Despite the relatively benign course of most viral cases of hepatitis, fulminant hepatitis represents a rare but feared complication. Fulminant hepatitis most commonly occurs in hepatitis B, D, and E. About 1–2% of cases of hepatitis E can lead to fulminant hepatitis, but pregnant women are particularly susceptible, occurring in up to 20% of cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Mortality rates in cases of fulminant hepatitis rise over 80%, but those patients that do survive often make a complete recovery. Liver transplantation can be life-saving in patients with fulminant liver failure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hepatitis D infections can transform benign cases of hepatitis B into severe, progressive hepatitis, a phenomenon known as <a href="/wiki/Superinfection" title="Superinfection">superinfection</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chronic_hepatitis_2">Chronic hepatitis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=58" title="Edit section: Chronic hepatitis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Acute hepatitis B infections become less likely to progress to chronic forms as the age of the patient increases, with rates of progression approaching 90% in vertically transmitted cases of infants compared to 1% risk in young adults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-37"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup> Overall, the 5-year survival rate for chronic hepatitis B ranges from 97% in mild cases to 55% in severe cases with cirrhosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-38"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most patients who acquire hepatitis D at the same time as hepatitis B (co-infection) recover without developing a chronic infection; however, in people with hepatitis B who later acquire hepatitis D (superinfection), chronic infection is much more common at 80-90%, and liver disease progression is accelerated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:48_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-:48-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<p>Chronic hepatitis C progresses towards cirrhosis, with estimates of cirrhosis prevalence of 16% at 20 years after infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> While the major causes of mortality in hepatitis C is end stage liver disease, hepatocellular carcinoma is an important additional long term complication and cause of death in chronic hepatitis.</p>
<p>Rates of mortality increase with progression of the underlying liver disease. Series of patients with compensated cirrhosis due to HCV have shown 3,5, and 10-year survival rates of 96, 91, and 79% respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> The 5-year survival rate drops to 50% upon if the cirrhosis becomes decompensated.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Epidemiology">Epidemiology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=59" title="Edit section: Epidemiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Viral_hepatitis_5">Viral hepatitis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=60" title="Edit section: Viral hepatitis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hepatitis_A_6">Hepatitis A</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=61" title="Edit section: Hepatitis A">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Hepatitis A is found throughout the world and manifests as large <a href="/wiki/Outbreak" title="Outbreak">outbreaks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Epidemic" title="Epidemic">epidemics</a> associated with fecal contamination of water and food sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-:7-90">[90]</a></sup> Hepatitis A viral infection is predominant in children ages 5–14 with rare infection of infants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_90-2"><a href="#cite_note-:7-90">[90]</a></sup> Infected children have little to no apparent clinical illness, in contrast to adults in whom greater than 80% are symptomatic if infected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> Infection rates are highest in low resource countries with inadequate public sanitation and large concentrated populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-49"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aggarwal_488.E2.80.93496_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aggarwal_488.E2.80.93496-111">[111]</a></sup> In such regions, as much as 90% of children younger than 10 years old have been infected and are immune, corresponding both to lower rates of clinically symptomatic disease and outbreaks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_90-3"><a href="#cite_note-:7-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aggarwal_488.E2.80.93496_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-Aggarwal_488.E2.80.93496-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> The availability of a childhood <a href="/wiki/Vaccine" title="Vaccine">vaccine</a> has significantly reduced infections in the United States, with incidence declining by more than 95% as of 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> Paradoxically, the highest rates of new infection now occur in young adults and adults who present with worse clinical illness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-50"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup> Specific populations at greatest risk include: travelers to endemic regions, men who have sex with men, those with occupational exposure to non-human primates, individuals with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clotting_disorder" title="Clotting disorder">clotting disorders</a> who have received <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clotting_factors" title="Clotting factors">clotting factors</a>, individuals with a history of <a href="/wiki/Chronic_liver_disease" title="Chronic liver disease">chronic liver disease</a> where co-infection with hepatitis A can lead to fulminant hepatitis, and intravenous drug users (rare).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_90-4"><a href="#cite_note-:7-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hepatitis_B_5">Hepatitis B</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=62" title="Edit section: Hepatitis B">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_B" title="Hepatitis B">Hepatitis B</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_B" title="Hepatitis B">Hepatitis B</a> is the most common cause of viral hepatitis in the world with more than 240 million chronic carriers of the virus, 1 million of whom are in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_25-3"><a href="#cite_note-:8-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_90-5"><a href="#cite_note-:7-90">[90]</a></sup> In approximately two-thirds of patients who develop acute hepatitis B infection, no identifiable exposure is evident.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-51"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup> Of those acutely infected, 25% become lifetime carriers of the virus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_90-6"><a href="#cite_note-:7-90">[90]</a></sup> Risk of infection is highest among intravenous drug users, individuals with high-risk sexual behaviors, healthcare workers, individuals with a history of multiple transfusions, organ transplant patients, dialysis patients and newborns infected during the birthing process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_90-7"><a href="#cite_note-:7-90">[90]</a></sup> Close to 780,000 deaths in the world are attributed to hepatitis B.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_25-4"><a href="#cite_note-:8-25">[25]</a></sup> The most endemic regions are in sub-Saharan Africa and East Asia where as much as 10% of adults are chronic carriers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_25-5"><a href="#cite_note-:8-25">[25]</a></sup> Carrier rates in developed nations are significantly lower, encompassing less than 1% of the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_25-6"><a href="#cite_note-:8-25">[25]</a></sup> In endemic regions, transmission is thought to be associated with exposure during birth and close contact between young infants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-52"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_25-7"><a href="#cite_note-:8-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hepatitis_C_4">Hepatitis C</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=63" title="Edit section: Hepatitis C">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_C" title="Hepatitis C">Hepatitis C</a></div>
<p>Chronic <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_C" title="Hepatitis C">hepatitis C</a> is a major cause of liver cirrhosis and hepatocellular carcinoma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-:12-114">[114]</a></sup> It is a common medical reason for liver transplantation due to its severe complications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-:12-114">[114]</a></sup> It is estimated that 130–180 million people in the world are affected by this disease representing a little more than 3% of the world population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:10_80-10"><a href="#cite_note-:10-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_90-8"><a href="#cite_note-:7-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_114-2"><a href="#cite_note-:12-114">[114]</a></sup> In the developing regions of Africa, Asia and South America, prevalence can be as high as 10% of the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_90-9"><a href="#cite_note-:7-90">[90]</a></sup> In Egypt, rates of hepatitis C infection as high as 20% have been documented and are associated with <a href="/wiki/Iatrogenesis" title="Iatrogenesis">iatrogenic</a> contamination related to <a href="/wiki/Schistosomiasis" title="Schistosomiasis">schistosomiasis</a> treatment in the 1950s–1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-53"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_90-10"><a href="#cite_note-:7-90">[90]</a></sup> Currently in the United States, approximately 3.5 million adults are estimated to be infected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> Hepatitis C is particularly prevalent among people born between 1945–1965, a group of about 800,000 people, with prevalence as high as 3.2% versus 1.6% in the general U.S. population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-54"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup> Most chronic carriers of hepatitis C are unaware of their infection status.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-55"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup> The most common mode of transmission of hepatitis C virus is exposure to blood products via blood transfusions (prior to 1992) and intravenous drug injection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-56"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_90-11"><a href="#cite_note-:7-90">[90]</a></sup> A history of intravenous drug injection is the most important risk factor for chronic hepatitis C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_114-3"><a href="#cite_note-:12-114">[114]</a></sup> Other susceptible populations include individuals with high-risk sexual behavior, infants of infected mothers, and healthcare workers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_90-12"><a href="#cite_note-:7-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hepatitis_D_3">Hepatitis D</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=64" title="Edit section: Hepatitis D">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_D" title="Hepatitis D">Hepatitis D</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_D" title="Hepatitis D">hepatitis D</a> virus causes chronic and fulminant hepatitis in the context of co-infection with the hepatitis B virus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_90-13"><a href="#cite_note-:7-90">[90]</a></sup> It is primarily transmitted via non-sexual contact and via needles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-57"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_90-14"><a href="#cite_note-:7-90">[90]</a></sup> Susceptibility to hepatitis D differs by geographic region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-58"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_90-15"><a href="#cite_note-:7-90">[90]</a></sup> In the United States and Northern Europe, populations at risk are intravenous drug users and individuals who receive multiple transfusions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-59"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_90-16"><a href="#cite_note-:7-90">[90]</a></sup> In the Mediterranean, hepatitis D is predominant among hepatitis B virus co-infected individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-60"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_90-17"><a href="#cite_note-:7-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hepatitis_E_3">Hepatitis E</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=65" title="Edit section: Hepatitis E">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_E" title="Hepatitis E">Hepatitis E</a></div>
<p>Similar to Hepatitis A, <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_E" title="Hepatitis E">hepatitis E</a> manifests as large outbreaks and epidemics associated with fecal contamination of water sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-61"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup> It accounts for more than 55,000 deaths annually with approximately 20 million people worldwide thought to be infected with the virus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:13_85-2"><a href="#cite_note-:13-85">[85]</a></sup> It affects predominantly young adults, causing acute hepatitis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-62"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-:6-116">[116]</a></sup> In infected pregnant women, Hepatitis E infection can lead to fulminant hepatitis with third trimester mortality rates as high as 30%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_90-18"><a href="#cite_note-:7-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-:6-116">[116]</a></sup> Individuals with weakened immune systems, such as organ transplant recipients, are also susceptible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_116-2"><a href="#cite_note-:6-116">[116]</a></sup> Infection is rare in the United States but rates are high in the developing world (Africa, Asia, Central America, Middle East).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-63"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_116-3"><a href="#cite_note-:6-116">[116]</a></sup> Many genotypes exist and are differentially distributed around the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:13_85-3"><a href="#cite_note-:13-85">[85]</a></sup> There is some evidence of hepatitis E infection of animals, serving as a reservoir for human infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_90-19"><a href="#cite_note-:7-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alcoholic_hepatitis_5">Alcoholic hepatitis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=66" title="Edit section: Alcoholic hepatitis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Alcoholic_hepatitis" title="Alcoholic hepatitis">Alcoholic hepatitis</a> (AH) in its severe form has a one-month mortality as high as 50%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:50_57-9"><a href="#cite_note-:50-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:58_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-:58-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:9_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-:9-117">[117]</a></sup> Most people who develop AH are men but women are at higher risk of developing AH and its complications likely secondary to high body fat and differences in alcohol metabolism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:58_58-2"><a href="#cite_note-:58-58">[58]</a></sup> Other contributing factors include younger age &lt;60, binge pattern drinking, poor nutritional status, obesity and hepatitis C co-infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:58_58-3"><a href="#cite_note-:58-58">[58]</a></sup> It is estimated that as much as 20% of people with AH are also infected with hepatitis C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:19_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-:19-118">[118]</a></sup> In this population, the presence of hepatitis C virus leads to more severe disease with faster progression to cirrhosis, hepatocellular carcinoma and increased mortality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:58_58-4"><a href="#cite_note-:58-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:19_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-:19-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> Obesity increases the likelihood of progression to cirrhosis in individuals with alcoholic hepatitis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:58_58-5"><a href="#cite_note-:58-58">[58]</a></sup> It is estimated that a high proportion of individuals (70%) who have AH will progress to cirrhosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:58_58-6"><a href="#cite_note-:58-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-alcoholic_steatohepatitis">Non-alcoholic steatohepatitis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=67" title="Edit section: Non-alcoholic steatohepatitis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Non-alcoholic steatohepatitis (NASH) is projected to become the top reason for <a href="/wiki/Liver_transplantation" title="Liver transplantation">liver transplantation</a> in the United States by the year 2020, supplanting chronic liver disease due to hepatitis C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:62_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-:62-120">[120]</a></sup> About 20–45% of the U.S. population have NAFLD and 6% have NASH.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:33_27-38"><a href="#cite_note-:33-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:37_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-:37-37">[37]</a></sup> The estimated prevalence of NASH in the world is 3–5%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:39_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-:39-121">[121]</a></sup> Of NASH patients who develop <a href="/wiki/Cirrhosis" title="Cirrhosis">cirrhosis</a>, about 2% per year will likely progress to <a href="/wiki/Hepatocellular_carcinoma" title="Hepatocellular carcinoma">hepatocellular carcinoma</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:39_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-:39-121">[121]</a></sup> Worldwide, the estimated prevalence of hepatocellular carcinoma related to NAFLD is 15–30%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:38_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-:38-122">[122]</a></sup> NASH is thought to be the primary cause of cirrhosis in approximately 25% of patients in the United States, representing 1–2% of the general population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:38_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-:38-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=68" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_thoughts">Early thoughts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=69" title="Edit section: Early thoughts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Initial accounts of a syndrome that we now think is likely to be hepatitis begin to occur around 3000 B.C. Clay tablets that served as medical handbooks for the ancient Sumerians described the first observations of jaundice. The Sumerians believed that the liver was the home of the soul, and attributed the findings of jaundice to the attack of one’s liver by a devil named Ahhazu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> Around 400 B.C., <a href="/wiki/Hippocrates" title="Hippocrates">Hippocrates</a> recorded the first documentation of an epidemic jaundice, in particular noting the uniquely fulminant course of a cohort of patients who all died within two weeks. He wrote, “The bile contained in the liver is full of phlegm and blood, and erupts...After such an eruption, the patient soon raves, becomes angry, talks nonsense and barks like a dog.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> His recommended treatment was a mixture of honey and water called <i>melikraton</i>.</p>
<p>Given the poor sanitary conditions of war, infectious jaundice played a large role as a major cause of mortality among troops in the Napoleonic Wars, the American Revolutionary War, and both World Wars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> During World War II, estimates of soldiers affected by hepatitis were upwards of 10 million. Soldiers received vaccines against diseases such as yellow fever, but these vaccines were stabilized with human serum and often created epidemics of hepatitis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> Researchers from England, Findlay and MacCallum, suspected these epidemics to be due to a separate infectious agent and not due to the yellow fever virus itself after noting 89 cases of jaundice in the months after vaccination out of a total 3,100 patients that they vaccinated. After changing the seed virus strain, they observed no cases of jaundice in the subsequent 8,000 vaccinations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Willowbrook_State_School_experiments">Willowbrook State School experiments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=70" title="Edit section: Willowbrook State School experiments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A New York University researcher named <a href="/wiki/Saul_Krugman" title="Saul Krugman">Saul Krugman</a> continued this research into the 1950s and 1960s, most infamously with his experiments on mentally disabled children at the Willowbrook State School in New York, a crowded urban facility where hepatitis infections were highly endemic to the student body. Krugman injected students with gamma globulin, a type of antibody. After observing the temporary protection against infection this antibody provided, he then tried injected live hepatitis virus into students. Krugman also controversially took feces from infected students, blended it into milkshakes, and fed it to newly admitted children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p>His research was received with much controversy, as people protested the questionable ethics surrounding the chosen target population. <a href="/wiki/Henry_K._Beecher" title="Henry K. Beecher">Henry Beecher</a> was one of the foremost critics in an article in the <i>New England Journal of Medicine</i> in 1966, arguing that parents were unaware to the risks of consent and that the research was done to benefit others at the expense of children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> Moreover, he argued that poor families with mentally disabled children often felt pressured to join the research project to gain admission to the school, with all of the educational and support resources that would come along with it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-130">[130]</a></sup> Others in the medical community spoke out in support of Krugman's research in terms of its widespread benefits and understanding of the hepatitis virus, and Willowbrook continues to be a commonly cited example in debates about medical ethics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
