<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Australia_antigen">The Australia antigen</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=71" title="Edit section: The Australia antigen">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The next leap forward in research was a serendipitous one by <a href="/wiki/Baruch_Samuel_Blumberg" title="Baruch Samuel Blumberg">Dr. Baruch Blumberg</a>, a researcher at the NIH who did not set out to research hepatitis, but rather studied lipoprotein genetics. He travelled across the globe collecting blood samples, investigating the interplay between disease, environment, and genetics with the goal of designing targeted interventions for at-risk individuals that could prevent them from getting sick.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> He noticed an unexpected interaction between the blood of a patient with <a href="/wiki/Haemophilia" title="Haemophilia">hemophilia</a> that had received multiple transfusions and a protein found in the blood of an Australian aborigine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> He named the protein the "Australia antigen" and made it the focus of his research. He found a higher prevalence of the protein in the blood of patients from developing countries, compared to those from developed ones, and noted associations of the antigen with other diseases like leukemia and Down Syndrome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> Eventually, he came to the unifying conclusion that the Australia antigen was associated with viral hepatitis. In 1970, <a href="/wiki/David_Dane" title="David Dane">David Dane</a> first isolated the hepatitis B <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Virion" title="Virion">virion</a> at London's Middlesex Hospital, and named the virion the 42-nm "Dane particle".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-130">[130]</a></sup> Based on its association with the surface of the hepatitis B virus, the Australia antigen was renamed to "hepatitis B surface antigen" or <a href="/wiki/HBsAg" title="HBsAg">HBsAg</a>. Blumberg continued to study the antigen, and eventually developed the first hepatitis B vaccine using plasma rich in HBsAg, for which he received the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physiology_or_Medicine" title="Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine">Nobel Prize in Medicine</a> in 1976.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_culture">Society and culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=72" title="Edit section: Society and culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_burden">Economic burden</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=73" title="Edit section: Economic burden">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Overall, hepatitis accounts for a significant portion of healthcare expenditures in both developing and developed nations, and is expected to rise in a several developing countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:25_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-:25-135">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:26_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-:26-136">[136]</a></sup> While hepatitis A infections are self-limited events, they are associated with significant costs in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:27_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-:27-137">[137]</a></sup> It has been estimated that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Direct_cost" title="Direct cost">direct and indirect costs</a> are approximately $1817 and $2459 respectively per case, and that an average of 27 work days is lost per infected adult.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:27_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-:27-137">[137]</a></sup> A 1997 report demonstrated that a single hospitalization related to hepatitis A cost an average of $6,900 and resulted in around $500 million in total annual healthcare costs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> Cost effectiveness studies have found widespread vaccination of adults to not be feasible, but have stated that a combination hepatitis A and B vaccination of children and at risk groups (people from endemic areas, healthcare workers) may be.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hepatitis B accounts for a much larger percentage of health care spending in endemic regions like Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:28_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-:28-141">[141]</a></sup> In 1997 it accounted for 3.2% of South Korea's total health care expenditures and resulted in $696 million in direct costs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:28_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-:28-141">[141]</a></sup> A large majority of that sum was spent on treating disease symptoms and complications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> Chronic hepatitis B infections are not as endemic in the United States, but accounted for $357 million in hospitalization costs in the year 1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:25_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-:25-135">[135]</a></sup> That number grew to $1.5 billion in 2003, but remained stable as of 2006, which may be attributable to the introduction of effective drug therapies and vaccination campaigns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:25_135-2"><a href="#cite_note-:25-135">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:26_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-:26-136">[136]</a></sup></p>
<p>People infected with chronic hepatitis C tend to be frequent users of the health care system globally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:29_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-:29-143">[143]</a></sup> It has been estimated that a person infected with hepatitis C in the United States will result in a monthly cost of $691.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:29_143-1"><a href="#cite_note-:29-143">[143]</a></sup> That number nearly doubles to $1,227 for people with compensated (stable) cirrhosis, while the monthly cost of people with decompensated (worsening) cirrhosis is almost five times as large at $3,682.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:29_143-2"><a href="#cite_note-:29-143">[143]</a></sup> The wide-ranging effects of hepatitis make it difficult to estimate indirect costs, but studies have speculated that the total cost is $6.5 billion annually in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:25_135-3"><a href="#cite_note-:25-135">[135]</a></sup> In Canada, 56% of HCV related costs are attributable to cirrhosis and total expenditures related to the virus are expected to peak at CAD$396 million in the year 2032.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_cases">Notable cases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=74" title="Edit section: Notable cases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The largest outbreak of hepatitis A virus in United States history occurred among people who ate at a now defunct <a href="/wiki/Mexican_cuisine" title="Mexican cuisine">Mexican food</a> restaurant located in Monaca, Pennsylvania in late 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:43_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-:43-145">[145]</a></sup> Over 550 people who visited the restaurant between September and October 2003 were infected with the virus, three of whom died as a direct result.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:43_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-:43-145">[145]</a></sup> The outbreak was brought to the attention of health officials when local <a href="/wiki/Emergency_medicine" title="Emergency medicine">emergency medicine</a> physicians noticed a significant increase in cases of hepatitis A in the county.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> After conducting its investigation, the <a href="/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention" title="Centers for Disease Control and Prevention">CDC</a> attributed the source of the outbreak to the use of contaminated raw <a href="/wiki/Scallion" title="Scallion">green onion</a>. The restaurant was purchasing its green onion stock from farms in Mexico at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:43_145-2"><a href="#cite_note-:43-145">[145]</a></sup> It is believed that the green onions may have been contaminated through the use of contaminated water for crop irrigation, rinsing, or icing or by handling of the vegetables by infected individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:43_145-3"><a href="#cite_note-:43-145">[145]</a></sup> Green onion had caused similar outbreaks of hepatitis A in the southern United States prior to this, but not to the same magnitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:43_145-4"><a href="#cite_note-:43-145">[145]</a></sup> The CDC believes that the restaurant's use of a large communal bucket for chopped raw green onion allowed non-contaminated plants to be mixed with contaminated ones, increasing the number of vectors of infection and amplifying the outbreak.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:43_145-5"><a href="#cite_note-:43-145">[145]</a></sup> The restaurant was closed once it was discovered to be the source, and over 9,000 people were given hepatitis A <a href="/wiki/Antibody" title="Antibody">immune globulin</a> because they had either eaten at the restaurant or had been in close contact with someone who had.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:43_145-6"><a href="#cite_note-:43-145">[145]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Research_directions">Research directions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=75" title="Edit section: Research directions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hepatitis_B_6">Hepatitis B</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=76" title="Edit section: Hepatitis B">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A Canadian paper published in 2015 used a mouse model of chronic hepatitis B infection and showed that interfering with certain proteins can facilitate clearance of the virus<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> which may have implications for human disease.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hepatitis_E_4">Hepatitis E</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=77" title="Edit section: Hepatitis E">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In March 2015, a group in China published an article regarding the development of a vaccine for hepatitis E.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> As of March 2016, the United States government was in the process of recruiting participants for the <a href="/wiki/Phases_of_clinical_research" title="Phases of clinical research">phase IV trial</a> of the drug Hecolin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Special_populations">Special populations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=78" title="Edit section: Special populations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="HIV_co-infection">HIV co-infection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=79" title="Edit section: HIV co-infection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Persons infected with HIV have a particularly high burden of <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_C_and_HIV_coinfection" title="Hepatitis C and HIV coinfection">HIV-HCV co-infection</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:57_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-:57-150">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:20_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-:20-151">[151]</a></sup> In a recent study by the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">WHO</a>, the likelihood of being infected with hepatitis C virus was six times greater in individuals who also had HIV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:20_151-1"><a href="#cite_note-:20-151">[151]</a></sup> The prevalence of HIV-HCV co-infection worldwide was estimated to be 6.2% representing more than 2.2 million people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:20_151-2"><a href="#cite_note-:20-151">[151]</a></sup> Intravenous drug use was an independent risk factor for HCV infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_114-4"><a href="#cite_note-:12-114">[114]</a></sup> In the WHO study, the prevalence of HIV-HCV co-infection was markedly higher at 82.4% in those who injected drugs compared to the general population (2.4%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:20_151-3"><a href="#cite_note-:20-151">[151]</a></sup> In a study of HIV-HCV co-infection among HIV positive men who have sex with men (MSM), the overall prevalence of anti-hepatitis C antibodies was estimated to be 8.1% and increased to 40% among HIV positive MSM who also injected drugs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:57_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-:57-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pregnancy">Pregnancy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=80" title="Edit section: Pregnancy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hepatitis_B_7">Hepatitis B</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=81" title="Edit section: Hepatitis B">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Vertically_transmitted_infection" title="Vertically transmitted infection">Vertical transmission</a> is a significant contributor of new <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_B_virus" title="Hepatitis B virus">HBV</a> cases each year, with 35–50% of transmission from mother to neonate in endemic countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:24_77-5"><a href="#cite_note-:24-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:44_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-:44-152">[152]</a></sup> Vertical transmission occurs largely via a neonate's exposure to maternal blood and vaginal secretions during birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:44_152-1"><a href="#cite_note-:44-152">[152]</a></sup> While the risk of progression to chronic infection is approximately 5% among adults who contract the virus, it is as high as 95% among neonates subject to vertical transmission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:24_77-6"><a href="#cite_note-:24-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> The risk of viral transmission is approximately 10–20% when maternal blood is positive for HBsAg, and up to 90% when also positive for <a href="/wiki/HBeAg" title="HBeAg">HBeAg</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:24_77-7"><a href="#cite_note-:24-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>Given the high risk of perinatal transmission, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centre_for_Disease_Control" title="Centre for Disease Control">CDC</a> recommends screening all pregnant women for HBV at their first prenatal visit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:24_77-8"><a href="#cite_note-:24-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> It is safe for non-immune pregnant women to receive the HBV vaccine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:24_77-9"><a href="#cite_note-:24-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:44_152-2"><a href="#cite_note-:44-152">[152]</a></sup> Based on the limited available evidence, the <a href="/wiki/American_Association_for_the_Study_of_Liver_Diseases" title="American Association for the Study of Liver Diseases">American Association for the Study of Liver Diseases</a> (AASLD) recommends antiviral therapy in pregnant women whose viral load exceeds 200,000 IU/mL.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:45_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-:45-155">[155]</a></sup> A growing body of evidence shows that antiviral therapy initiated in the third trimester significantly reduces transmission to the neonate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:44_152-3"><a href="#cite_note-:44-152">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:45_155-1"><a href="#cite_note-:45-155">[155]</a></sup> A systematic review of the Antiretroviral Pregnancy Registry database found that there was no increased risk of congenital anomalies with <a href="/wiki/Tenofovir_disoproxil" title="Tenofovir disoproxil">Tenofovir</a>; for this reason, along with its potency and low risk of resistance, the AASLD recommends this drug.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:45_155-2"><a href="#cite_note-:45-155">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> A 2010 systematic review and meta-analysis found that <a href="/wiki/Lamivudine" title="Lamivudine">Lamivudine</a> initiated early in the third trimester also significantly reduced mother-to-child transmission of HBV, without any known adverse effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/American_Congress_of_Obstetricians_and_Gynecologists" title="American Congress of Obstetricians and Gynecologists">ACOG</a> states that the evidence available does not suggest any particular mode of delivery (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Vaginal_delivery" title="Vaginal delivery">vaginal</a> vs. <a href="/wiki/Caesarean_section" title="Caesarean section">cesarean</a>) is better at reducing vertical transmission in mothers with HBV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:24_77-10"><a href="#cite_note-:24-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">WHO</a> and CDC recommend that neonates born to mothers with HBV should receive hepatitis B immune globulin (<a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_B_immune_globulin" title="Hepatitis B immune globulin">HBIG</a>) as well as the <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_B_vaccine" title="Hepatitis B vaccine">HBV vaccine</a> within 12 hours of birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:21_68-15"><a href="#cite_note-:21-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:23_76-10"><a href="#cite_note-:23-76">[76]</a></sup> For infants who have received HBIG and the HBV vaccine, breastfeeding is safe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:24_77-11"><a href="#cite_note-:24-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:44_152-4"><a href="#cite_note-:44-152">[152]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hepatitis_C_5">Hepatitis C</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=82" title="Edit section: Hepatitis C">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Estimates of the rate of <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_C_virus" title="Hepatitis C virus">HCV</a> vertical transmission range from 2–8%; a 2014 systematic review and meta-analysis found the risk to be 5.8% in HCV-positive, HIV-negative women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:24_77-12"><a href="#cite_note-:24-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:47_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-:47-158">[158]</a></sup> The same study found the risk of vertical transmission to be 10.8% in HCV-positive, HIV-positive women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:47_158-1"><a href="#cite_note-:47-158">[158]</a></sup> Other studies have found the risk of vertical transmission to be as high as 44% among HIV-positive women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:24_77-13"><a href="#cite_note-:24-77">[77]</a></sup> The risk of vertical transmission is higher when the virus is detectable in the mother's blood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:47_158-2"><a href="#cite_note-:47-158">[158]</a></sup></p>
<p>Evidence does not indicate that mode of delivery (i.e. vaginal vs. cesarean) has an effect on vertical transmission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:24_77-14"><a href="#cite_note-:24-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>For women who are HCV-positive and HIV-negative, breastfeeding is safe; however, CDC guidelines suggest avoiding breastfeeding if a woman's nipples are "cracked or bleeding" to reduce the risk of transmission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:24_77-15"><a href="#cite_note-:24-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:53_79-8"><a href="#cite_note-:53-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hepatitis_E_5">Hepatitis E</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=83" title="Edit section: Hepatitis E">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Pregnant women who contract <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_E_virus" title="Hepatitis E virus">HEV</a> are at significant risk of developing fulminant hepatitis with maternal mortality rates as high as 20–30%, most commonly in the third trimester .<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-64"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:24_77-16"><a href="#cite_note-:24-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:44_152-5"><a href="#cite_note-:44-152">[152]</a></sup> A 2016 systematic review and meta-analysis of 47 studies that included 3968 people found maternal <a href="/wiki/Case_fatality_rate" title="Case fatality rate">case-fatality rates</a> (CFR) of 20.8% and fetal CFR of 34.2%; among women who developed fulminant hepatic failure, CFR was 61.2%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hepatitis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=84" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/World_Hepatitis_Day" title="World Hepatitis Day">World Hepatitis Day</a></li>
</ul>
